Fifth Disagreement: Can Substance Be Intense?
Author discusses whether substance can be intense, contrasting Illuminationist and Peripatetic views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Illuminationist argument that substance can be intense based on the perfection of essence, and the Peripatetic reliance on convention.

Core Idea
Substance can be intense because its essence can be more or less perfect, as seen in the difference between an elephant and a mosquito.

The fifth disagreement asks whether substance (jawhar) can have intensity. The Illuminationists affirm, defining intensity as perfection of essence; hence a perfect substance like an elephant's is more intense than a mosquito's. The Peripatetics deny without proof, relying on linguistic convention. The author implies that even on Peripatetic principles, substance can receive increase and decrease.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Illuminationist claim
The Illuminationists assert that substance can be intense. This follows from their definition of intensity as perfection of essence.
2
Understand their argument
They provide an example: the substantial essence in an elephant is more perfect than that in a mosquito, due to the more extensive effects of the elephant. Therefore, the elephant's substance is more intense.
The argument ties intensity to observable effects (āthār).
3
Note the Peripatetic denial
The Peripatetics simply deny that substance can be intense, but they do not provide a proof. Their denial is based on common parlance, where one does not say a substance is more intense than another.
The author points out the lack of demonstration (dalīl).
4
Recognize the author's point about consistency
The author notes that even on Peripatetic principles, bodily magnitude (al-miqdār al-jismānī) is considered a substance and yet it can increase and decrease. Thus, their own system allows for intensity in substance.
This reveals an internal inconsistency in the Peripatetic position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاختلاف الرابع: قال الإشراقيون الزيادة والقوة والشدة أمر واحد، كمال الماهية ، لكن إذا وجدت في الكم سميت زيادة، وإذا وجدت في الجوهر سميت قوة، وإذا وجدت في الكيف سميت شدة، وكذا أضدادها، لكن هذه إطلاقات عرفية لا اعتداد بها في العلوم الحكمية، والمشاؤون زلوا وقالوا بتغايرها ؛ نظرا إلى الإطلاقات، وناقضوا أنفسهم حيث لم يجوزوا كون الخط أشد خطية ، وجوزوا كونه أشد طولا ، مع أن الطول هو الخط، والمصنف اختار مذهب الإشراقية ؛ حيث عرف الشدة بما يتناول الزيادة أيضا. الاختلاف الخامس أن الجوهر هل يشتد ، أم لا ؟ قال الإشراقيون: نعم، وهو ظاهر على أصلهم ؛ فإنهم عنوا بها كمال الماهية، والماهية الجوهرية في الفيل أكمل من البعوضة ؛ لظهور الآثار الكثيرة في الفيل دونها، وعلى ما فسره أتباع المشائين له تجويز أيضا ؛ فإن من الجائز أن يكون بعض المفارقات بحيث ينتزع عنه أمثال مفارق آخر، ولم يدل دليل على خلافه، وقد ادعى الإشراقيون فيه المشاهدة بالرياضيات، وقال المشاؤون: لا يشتد الجوهر، ولم أنه يقيموا عليه دليلا بل بنوا على مجرى العرف ؛ حيث لم يطلق على جوهر أشد من جوهر آخر ، هذا والله أعلم بالصواب. والزيادة والنقصان على أصلهم أيضا يتصف الجوهر بهما فإنهم قالوا المقدار الجسماني جوهر وعين الجسم، مع أنه يتصف بهما)، والله أعلم بحقيقة الحال . (وَإِنْ كَثُرَ) معناه ، (فَإِنْ وُضِعَ ذلك المفرد (لِكُلِّ [مِنَ الْمَعَانِي ] (۲))
The fourth disagreement: The Illuminationists said: increase (ziyāda), strength (quwwa), and intensity (shidda) are one thing, the perfection of the essence, but when found in quantity it is called increase, when found in substance it is called strength, and when found in quality it is called intensity, and similarly their opposites. However, these are conventional expressions not to be relied upon in the philosophical sciences. The Peripatetics erred and said they are differentiated based on these conventional usages, and they contradicted themselves when they did not allow a line to be more intense in line-ness, but allowed it to be more intense in length, even though length is the line. The author chose the Illuminationist school, because he defined intensity in a way that includes increase as well. The fifth disagreement: whether substance can be intense or not. The Illuminationists said yes, and it is evident according to their principle, because they intended by it the perfection of the essence, and the substantial essence in an elephant is more perfect than that in a mosquito due to the appearance of many effects in the elephant and not in it. And according to the interpretation of the followers of the Peripatetics, it is also permissible; for it is possible that some separate entities are such that similar separate forms can be extracted from them, and no proof to the contrary. The Illuminationists claimed direct observation through spiritual exercises. The Peripatetics said that substance does not become intense, but they did not establish a proof for it; rather they relied on conventional usage, since they do not say a substance is more intense than another. And Allah knows best what is correct. And increase and decrease according to their principle also apply to substance, because they said that bodily magnitude is a substance and identical to the body, while it can have increase and decrease. And Allah knows the reality of the matter. (And if it increases) its meaning, (then if that singular term is placed for each of the meanings) ...
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage covers two more disagreements. The fourth concerns the relationship between increase, strength, and intensity. The Illuminationists consider them identical as perfection of essence, only differing by context: in quantity it's increase, in substance strength, in quality intensity. The Peripatetics mistakenly differentiated them based on conventional language, leading to self-contradiction: they deny that a line can be more intense in line-ness but affirm it can be more intense in length, although length is the line. The author (of the matn) sided with the Illuminationists by including increase in the definition of intensity. The fifth disagreement asks whether substance can be intense. The Illuminationists affirm, using the perfection of essence: an elephant's substantial essence is more perfect than a mosquito's due to its more extensive effects. Even on the Peripatetics' interpretation, it is possible if some separate entities can be mentally analyzed into weaker ones. The Peripatetics deny it without proof, relying on common usage. The passage ends with a remark that, on the Peripatetics' own principles, substance can be described with increase and decrease since bodily magnitude is a substance yet admits them. The final fragment appears to be a continuation of the commentary on the matn's definition of 'if it increases'.

Key Terms
الجوهر (al-jawhar)
الشدة (al-shidda)
الكمال (al-kamāl)
الآثار (al-āthār)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Peripatetics, why do they deny that substance can be intense?
A) Because it contradicts the definition of substance
B) Because they have a logical proof against it
C) Because it is not customary to say a substance is more intense than another
D) Because they follow the Illuminationists

Key Takeaway
The Peripatetics' denial of intensity in substance is based on linguistic convention, not demonstration, leaving room for the Illuminationist view that substance can be more or less perfect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Identify the underlying principle (intensity as perfection of essence) and evaluate whether the example successfully demonstrates that intensity in substance is real. Could the example be countered by the Peripatetics?
?
The author points out that the Peripatetics, despite denying intensity in substance, admit that bodily magnitude (a substance) can increase and decrease. Show how this creates an inconsistency in their position.

Murtajal: Definition and Classification Debate
Author defines murtajal and its classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the definition of murtajal and distinguish it from mushtarak based on the three opinions presented in the text.

Core Idea
Murtajal is a transfer without suitability, making it look like a new coinage for each meaning.

In this passage, the author defines 'murtajal' as a word transferred from one meaning to another with no suitability between them. This leads to a debate: is murtajal a type of mushtarak, or a separate category? We will unpack the three opinions and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Find the definition of murtajal
Locate the defining statement: a word transferred from original to another without suitability. Identify the key phrase 'من غير مناسبة'.
2
Notice the three opinions
The text gives three views: (a) it is from mushtarak, (b) it is its correlative (qasim), (c) it is from manqul. Underline these phrases.
The three opinions are separated by brackets in the Arabic.
3
Understand the reasoning behind each
Why would lack of suitability make it seem like mushtarak? Because it appears as if coined for each meaning independently. Why does the majority consider it a distinct category? Because transfer still occurs, even without suitability.
The third opinion links murtajal to manqul based on transfer.
4
Apply the definition to examples
Think of a word like 'ayn that has multiple unrelated meanings. Would it be murtajal or mushtarak? Use the text's criteria: is there transfer without suitability?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالْمُرْتَجَلُ) ، وهو اللفظ المنقول عن معناه الأصلي إلى آخر، من غير مناسبة (قِيلَ مِنَ الْمُشْتَرَكِ) ؛ لأنه لما لم يكن بين المنقول عنه والمنقول إليه مناسبة فكأنه وضع لكل منهما ابتداء، والجمهور على أنه قسيم له، [وَقِيلَ مِنَ الْمَنْقُولِ ] . (وَإِلَّا) يكن وضعه لهما ابتداء ، بل للأول فقط، (فَإِنِ اشْتَهَرَ فِي الثَّاني) المنقول إليه، بأن يفهم من غير قرينة (فَمَنْقُولُ: شَرْعِي)، إن كان الناقل الشرع، أَوْ عُرْفِيٌّ: خَاصّ)، إن كان صاحب عرف خاص، (أو) [عُرْفِي] (عام)، إن كان عرفا عاما . قَالَ سِيبَوَيْهِ : (الْأَعْلامُ كُلُّها منقولات). [ خِلَافًا لِلْجُمْهُور ] من النحاة .
And al-murtajal: it is the word transferred from its original meaning to another, without suitability. It is said to be from al-mushtarak, because since there is no suitability between the transferred-from and the transferred-to, it is as if it was coined for each from the beginning. The majority hold that it is its correlative (qasim). And it is said to be from al-manqul. And if it is not coined for both, but only for the first, then if it becomes famous in the second (the transferred-to), such that it is understood without a clue, then it is manqul: shar'i, if the transferor is the Law; or 'urfi khas, if it is from a specific custom; or 'urfi 'amm, if it is a general custom. Sibawayh said: 'All proper names are transferred.' Contrary to the majority of grammarians.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage defines murtajal and discusses its classification. The author begins by defining murtajal as a word transferred from its original meaning to another without any suitability or relation between the two meanings. Because of the lack of suitability, it is as if the word was coined for each meaning independently. Hence some say it falls under mushtarak (homonym). However, the majority (jumhur) consider it a distinct category (qasim) alongside mushtarak. Then the passage presents a third opinion that it is a type of manqul (transferred word). The discussion then moves to manqul proper: if the word is originally coined for only one meaning, and then it becomes famous (ishtahara) in a secondary meaning such that it is understood without a contextual clue, then it is called manqul. It is subdivided into shar'i (legal transfer), 'urfi khas (specific customary transfer), and 'urfi 'amm (general customary transfer). The passage also notes Sibawayh's statement that all proper names (a'lam) are transferred, but the majority of grammarians disagree.

Key Terms
المرتجل
المنقول
المشترك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority (al-jumhur), what is the relationship between al-murtajal and al-mushtarak?
A) Al-murtajal is a type of al-mushtarak.
B) Al-murtajal is a distinct category alongside al-mushtarak.
C) Al-murtajal is a type of al-manqul.
D) Al-murtajal is a type of al-majaz.

Key Takeaway
Murtajal is a transfer without suitability, leading to debate whether it is mushtarak, its correlative, or a type of manqul.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the word 'zakat'. In Arabic, it originally meant 'purity' or 'growth'. In Islamic law, it refers to obligatory charity. Classify it as murtajal or manqul based on the definitions in this lesson. Which opinion on murtajal (if any) would apply?
?
Find another example in Islamic terminology (e.g., 'hajj', 'sawm') and determine whether it is murtajal or manqul, explaining your reasoning using the criteria from the passage.

Manqul: Types of Transfer and Disagreement on Proper Names
Author defines manqul and its subtypes, with Sibawayh's view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the definition of manqul, list its three subtypes (shar'i, 'urfi khas, 'urfi 'amm), and analyze the disagreement about proper names.

Core Idea
Manqul is a transfer that becomes so famous it needs no clue.

The passage continues by defining manqul: a word coined for one meaning then transferred to another, becoming famous in the second meaning. It is subdivided by the source of transfer: Law, specific custom, or general custom. Additionally, Sibawayh's claim about proper names being manqul is contrasted with the majority of grammarians.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for manqul
Look for the phrase 'فإن اشتهر في الثاني بأن يفهم من غير قرينة'. The word must become famous in the second meaning so that it is understood without a contextual clue.
Without fame, it might be a metaphor or a less common usage.
2
List the three subdivisions
Find: 'فمنقول: شرعي' if the transferor is the Law; 'عرفي خاص' if from a specific custom; 'عرفي عام' if from a general custom. Note the examples: 'salat' for shar'i, local terms for 'urfi.
These subdivisions are based on the source of transfer.
3
Understand Sibawayh's claim
He said 'الأعلام كلها منقولات' meaning all proper names are transferred. Many proper names originated as common nouns (e.g., 'Asad' = lion). The majority of grammarians disagreed, possibly because they consider proper names as original coinages.
4
Analyze the disagreement
Think about why the majority opposed Sibawayh. What assumption about proper names do they hold? Does the definition of manqul require fame? Many proper names are not famously transferred from a previous meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And al-murtajal: it is the word transferred from its original meaning to another, without suitability. And if it is not coined for both, but only for the first, then if it becomes famous in the second (the transferred-to), such that it is understood without a clue, then it is manqul: shar'i, if the transferor is the Law; or 'urfi khas, if it is from a specific custom; or 'urfi 'amm, if it is a general custom. Sibawayh said: 'All proper names are transferred.' Contrary to the majority of grammarians.

Commentary
This passage defines murtajal and discusses its classification. The author begins by defining murtajal as a word transferred from its original meaning to another without any suitability or relation between the two meanings. Because of the lack of suitability, it is as if the word was coined for each meaning independently. Hence some say it falls under mushtarak (homonym). However, the majority (jumhur) consider it a distinct category (qasim) alongside mushtarak. Then the passage presents a third opinion that it is a type of manqul (transferred word). Haqiqa and Majaz: Conditions and Types
Author defines haqiqa and majaz, and divides majaz into isti'ara and mursal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish haqiqa from majaz based on the presence of a clue (qarina) and a relation (alaka), and classify a given majaz as isti'ara or mursal.

Core Idea
A word's status as haqiqa or majaz depends on usage, not inherent nature.

Understanding haqiqa (literal) and majaz (figurative) is essential for interpreting the Qur'an and Islamic texts. This passage clarifies the conditions: a word is haqiqa if used in its original meaning without a diverting clue; if a clue is needed, it becomes majaz. Majaz further divides based on the relation between meanings: tashbih (similitude) gives isti'ara, while other relations give majaz mursal. Let’s work through this step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core distinction
The passage states that whether a word is haqiqa or majaz depends on usage and the need for a clue. If used in its original meaning without a clue diverting from it, it is haqiqa. If used in a secondary meaning requiring a clue to divert from the original, it is majaz. Note that a word before any usage is neither.
2
Understand the role of the clue (qarina)
A clue (qarina) is necessary for majaz to signal that the secondary meaning is intended. Without it, the literal meaning is assumed.
The clue can be linguistic or contextual.
3
Examine the relation (alaka)
For majaz to occur, there must be a relation between the original and secondary meanings. The passage specifies that if the relation is tashbih (similitude)—sharing a significant attribute—it is called isti'ara (metaphor). Otherwise, it is majaz mursal.
Majaz mursal has 24 possible relations, such as causality, part-whole, etc.
4
Apply to examples
Consider the Arabic word 'asad' (lion). Literally, it means the animal (haqiqa). When used for a brave man, it is majaz. The relation is tashbih (bravery shared), so it's isti'ara. Now consider 'yad' (hand) used for power; the relation might be causality or part-whole, so it is majaz mursal.
Islamic examples from tafsir and usul al-fiqh will reinforce this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِلَّا بقرينة (اشتهر في الثاني المنقول إليه، بل يحتاج في فهمه إلى صارفة عن الأول، (فَحَقِيقَةٌ) في الأول ، إن استعمل فيه، (ومجاز) في الثاني، إن استعمل فيه فالمفرد قبل الاستعمال لا حقيقة ولا مجاز ، ولا بد من مجاز ( عَلَاقَةِ) بين المعنيين؛ لينتقل إلى الثاني الغير المشتهر، (فإن كانت) تلك العلاقة في المجاز (تَشْبِيهَا)، وهو المشاركة في وصف معتد به (فاستعارة) . أي فالمجاز استعارة، (وَإِلَّا فَمَجَازٌ مُرْسَلٌ وَحصرُوهُ أي المجاز المرسل علاقات باعتبار العلاقة (في أَرْبَعَةِ وَعِشْرِينَ نَوْعًا)
And if not, with a clue (it became famous in the second the transferred-to, but rather it needs a clue diverting from the first), then it is haqiqa in the first, if used therein, and majaz in the second, if used therein. So a word before usage is neither haqiqa nor majaz. There must be a relation (alaka) between the two meanings to transfer to the second which is not famous. Then if that relation in the majaz is tashbih (similitude), i.e., sharing in a significant attribute, then it is isti'ara. So a majaz is isti'ara. Otherwise, it is majaz mursal. And they restricted it, i.e., majaz mursal, according to the relation, to twenty-four types.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage defines the conditions for haqiqa and majaz. If the word did not become famous in the second meaning (i.e., it is not manqul), but requires a clue to divert from the original, then when used in the original meaning it is haqiqa, and when used in the new meaning it is majaz. Before any usage, a word is neither haqiqa nor majaz. For majaz to occur, there must be a relation (alaka) between the two meanings to enable the transfer. This relation is either similitudinous (tashbih), i.e., the two meanings share a significant attribute, in which case the majaz is called isti'ara (metaphor). If the relation is other than tashbih, it is called majaz mursal (unrestricted metaphor), which has been categorized into twenty-four types based on the specific relation.

Key Terms
الحقيقة
المجاز
الاستعارة
المجاز المرسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is required for a majaz to be considered isti'ara?
A) The relation must be causality (sababiyya)
B) The relation must be part-whole (juz'iyya)
C) The relation must be tashbih (similitude)
D) The relation must be opposition (taddad)

Key Takeaway
A word's classification as haqiqa or majaz depends on usage, and majaz is further divided into isti'ara (relation of similitude) and mursal (other relations).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

For the majaz, classify as isti'ara or mursal and state the relation.
?
In the Qur'anic verse 'فَاصْبِرْ كَمَا صَبَرَ أُولُو الْعَزْمِ مِنَ الرُّسُلِ' (So be patient as were those of resolve among the messengers), identify the type of majaz if any, and the relation.

Distinguishing Haqiqa and Majaz
Author defines haqiqa and majaz with conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between haqiqa (literal) and majaz (figurative) usage based on original meaning, contextual clue, and relation.

Core Idea
A word's meaning is not fixed; it becomes literal or figurative based on use and context.

The classification of words into haqiqa and majaz is foundational in Islamic sciences. This lesson clarifies the definitions and conditions laid out in the passage from Bahr Ulum, which explains that a word before usage is neither, and that figurative usage requires a relation (alaqa) and a contextual clue (qarina).

Guided Path
1
Identify the original meaning (wad')
Determine the original, first-established meaning of the word as per linguistic convention.
2
Check the actual usage
Is the word used in that original meaning? If yes, it is haqiqa. If not, proceed to the next step.
3
Look for a contextual clue (qarina)
Find a word or context that signals that the literal meaning is not intended, diverting to a figurative meaning.
The passage states that a qarina is needed to understand the figurative meaning.
4
Verify the relation (alaqa)
There must be a relation between the literal and figurative meanings that justifies the transfer. Without alaqa, the usage is not valid majaz.
The alaqa is the basis for classifying the type of majaz.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الألفاظ
الشرع، أَوْ عُرْفِيٌّ: خَاصّ)، إن كان صاحب عرف خاص، (أو) عُرْفِي عام)، إن كان عرفا عاما . قَالَ سِيبَوَيْهِ: (الْأَعْلامُ كُلُّها منقولات). خِلَافًا لِلْجُمْهُور من النحاة .
(وَإِلَّا) قرينة اشتهر في الثاني المنقول إليه، بل يحتاج في فهمه إلى صارفة عن الأول، (فَحَقِيقَةٌ) في الأول ، إن استعمل فيه، (ومجاز) في الثاني، إن استعمل فيه فالمفرد قبل الاستعمال لا حقيقة ولا مجاز ، ولا بد من مجاز(4) ( عَلَاقَةِ) بين المعنيين؛ لينتقل إلى الثاني الغير المشتهر، (فإن كانت) تلك العلاقة في المجاز (تَشْبِيهَا)، وهو المشاركة في وصف معتد به (فاستعارة) . أي فالمجاز استعارة، (وَإِلَّا فَمَجَازٌ مُرْسَلٌ وَحصرُوهُ أي المجاز المرسل علاقات باعتبار العلاقة (في أَرْبَعَةِ وَعِشْرِينَ نَوْعًا):
(۱) السببية.
(۲) والمسببية .
(۳) والكلية .
(٤) والجزئية .
(٥) واللازمية.
(٦) والملزومية.
(۷) والتقييد .
(۸) والإطلاق.
(۹) والعموم.
(١٠) والخصوص.
(۱۱) والمجاورة.
(۱۲) والأولُ إليه.
(۱۳) والكون فيه .
(١٤) والحالية.
١٥) والمحلية .
(١٦) وآلية الشيء.
(۱۷) وبدلية أحدهما عن الآخر.
(۱۸) والتضاد .
(۱۹) والنكرة الواقعة في حيز الإثبات العامة.
(۲۰) والمعرف باللام للعهد الذهني.
ولا يشترط، وهو هكذا مشهورة في كلام السيد السند وغيره، ولا أظنه من المتن.
Chapter on Words: [A word is] either legislative (shar'i) or customary (urfi): specific if it has a specific custom, or general if the custom is general. Sibawayh said: 'All proper nouns are transferred.' Contrary to the majority of grammarians. [If not,] there is a contextual clue (qarina) that is known in the second [meaning] to which it is transferred; rather it needs in understanding it a diverting [clue] from the first. So it is literal (haqiqa) in the first [meaning] if used therein, and figurative (majaz) in the second if used therein. Thus the single word before usage is neither literal nor figurative. There must be a relation (alaqa) between the two meanings to transfer to the second [meaning] that is not well-known. If that relation in the figurative [usage] is resemblance—which is participation in an admissible attribute—then it is metaphor (isti'ara). That is, the figurative [usage] is metaphor. Otherwise, it is unrestricted figurative (majaz mursal). They restricted it—i.e., unrestricted figurative—to twenty-four types based on the relation: (1) causality, (2) effectuality, (3) totality, (4) partiality, (5) necessity, (6) necessitated, (7) restriction, (8) absoluteness, (9) generality, (10) specificity, (11) adjacency, (12) priority to it, (13) being in it, (14) state, (15) location, (16) instrumentality of the thing, (17) substitution of one for the other, (18) opposition, (19) indefinite noun in positive context with generality, (20) definite noun with the lam for mental covenant. 'And it is not stipulated' — it is thus well-known in the speech of al-Sayyid al-Sanad and others, but I do not think it is from the text.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage from Bahr Ulum is a definitional and classificatory section on words (alfaz). It begins by categorizing words based on their origin: either legislative (shar'i) or customary (urfi), the latter further divided into specific and general. The author cites Sibawayh's statement that all proper nouns are transferred from original meanings. Then the passage discusses literal and figurative usage: a word before use is neither; it becomes literal when used in its original meaning and figurative when used in a transferred meaning, provided there is a relation (alaqa) between them. If the word is commonly known in the figurative sense, a contextual clue (qarina) is needed to divert from the literal. The key division is that if the relation is resemblance (tashbih), the figurative usage is called isti'ara (metaphor); otherwise it is majaz mursal (unrestricted figurative). The passage then enumerates 24 types of majaz mursal based on the specific relation, listing 20 examples such as causality (e.g., using 'rain' for the resulting vegetation), effectuality (using 'produce' for the rain), and others. In Islamic sciences, these help interpret texts like the Qur'an: for instance, addressing the hand of Allah (a part for the whole) is a type of majaz mursal (partiality). The commentary also notes a remark that a certain condition is not required, citing al-Sayyid al-Sanad.

Key Terms
حقيقة
مجاز
علاقة
قرينة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what must exist for a word to be considered majaz?
A) The word must be a proper noun.
B) There must be a relation (alaqa) and a contextual clue (qarina).
C) The word must be used in its original meaning.
D) The word must be a metaphor (isti'ara).

Key Takeaway
Haqiqa and majaz are determined by usage, not by the word itself; the presence of a relation and a contextual clue are necessary for majaz.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a word in a Qur'anic verse that is commonly understood figuratively (e.g., 'hand' in 'hand of Allah'). Determine whether it is haqiqa or majaz, and state the alaqa and qarina used.
?
Explain why the word before usage is neither haqiqa nor majaz, using an example from Islamic law.

Isti'ara and Majaz Mursal: The Role of Resemblance
Author divides majaz into isti'ara and majaz mursal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any figurative expression as isti'ara (metaphor) or majaz mursal (unrestricted) based on whether the relation involves resemblance.

Core Idea
Resemblance turns majaz into metaphor; all other relations produce majaz mursal.

The passage divides majaz into two broad categories: isti'ara, where the relation is resemblance, and majaz mursal, for all other relations. This lesson focuses on that division, helping you identify which category a given figurative usage falls into.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figurative usage
Locate a word used not in its original meaning.
2
Extract the relation (alaqa) between literal and figurative meanings
What connects the two meanings? For example, causality, part-whole, or resemblance in an attribute.
3
Check if the relation is resemblance (tashbih)
Resemblance means the two share a common attribute. If yes, the figurativ e usage is isti'ara. If the relation is any other kind, it is majaz mursal.
Resemblance is defined as 'participation in an admissible attribute'.
4
Classify the expression
Label it as isti'ara if resemblance is present; otherwise, it is majaz mursal and can be further subtyped.

The Twenty-Four Types of Majaz Mursal
Author enumerates 20 types of majaz mursal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the type of relation in a given majaz mursal expression from the list of 20 (or 24) types.

Core Idea
The relations in majaz mursal are diverse, from causality to opposition, covering many linguistic transfers.

The passage enumerates 24 types of majaz mursal based on the relation between literal and figurative meanings. Although only 20 are listed here, they represent the main categories used in Islamic sciences. This lesson helps you recognize and apply these types to examples from the Qur'an and Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Familiarize yourself with the list of relations
Read the 20 listed types: causality, effectuality, totality, partiality, etc. Note that the full list is 24.
Common types include causality (using cause for effect), totality (whole for part), and partiality (part for whole).
2
For each type, find a familiar example
Connect each relation to a known figurative expression from Islamic texts.
E.g., 'And ask the village' (12:82) – the village is used for its inhabitants (relation of being in it or containment).
3
Given a new figurative expression, identify its relation
Determine what connects the literal and figurative meanings. Is it cause-effect? Part-whole? Resemblance would exclude it from majaz mursal.
4
Classify it as the appropriate type of majaz mursal
Name the specific type (e.g., causality, partiality) from the list.
If the relation is not in the list, it may be a missing type from the 24.

Classification and Critique of Deletion Types in Metaphor
Author enumerates four types of deletion/addition and critiques their separate counting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four types of deletion and addition in metaphor and explain why the author considers separate counting of specific deletions as redundant.

Core Idea
Not all deletions are independent categories: absolute deletion already includes specific ones.

In Islamic rhetoric and logic, precise classification is essential for clear analysis. This passage presents four types of deletion and addition used in metaphorical expressions, but then critiques the practice of listing specific deletions separately when they fall under a more general category. Understanding this critique sharpens your ability to evaluate logical classifications and avoid redundancy.

Guided Path
1
Enumerate the four types
Identify the four numbered items: deletion of the mudaf (annexed), deletion of the mudaf ilayhi (annexed-to), absolute deletion, and addition.
These are types of figurative language in Arabic rhetoric.
2
Understand the critique
The author says counting the last five (likely including these four) is disputable. Specifically, counting deletion of mudaf and mudaf ilayhi separately is 'not commendable'.
The phrase 'غير مستحسن' indicates disapproval.
3
Identify the reason
The author provides the reason: 'مع دخوله في الحذف مطلقا' — these specific deletions are already included under absolute deletion.
This is a logical classification principle: avoid overlapping categories.
4
Apply to broader classification
Consider how this principle applies to other Islamic sciences. For example, in usul al-fiqh, a general ruling (like 'all intoxicants are forbidden') should not be repeated for each specific intoxicant.
This highlights the importance of hierarchical categorization.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الألفاظ
(۲۱) وحذف المضاف .
(۲۲) وحذف المضاف إليه.
(۲۳) والحذف مطلقا .
(٢٤) والزيادة (١) .
ثم في عد الخمسة الأخيرة كلام، أما الأول فلأنه وإن لم يكن اللام المستعمل في العهد الذهني حقيقة ، على ما هو التحقيق، لكن العلاقة فيه تشبيه الواحد الغير المعين بالمعين، فدخل في الاستعارة. والأربعة الأخيرة ليست من علاقات المجاز اللغوي المذكور ههنا وإن كان قد يطلق المجاز بالحذف على ما قدر فيه شيء وأقيم آخر مقامه في الإعراب، والمجاز بالزيادة على ما زيد. ثم عَدُّ حذف المضاف والمضاف إليه على حدة، مع دخوله في الحذف مطلقا غير مستحسن.
Section on words. (21) Deletion of the annexed (mudaf). (22) Deletion of the annexed-to (mudaf ilayhi). (23) Deletion absolutely. (24) Addition. Then regarding counting the last five, there is discussion. As for the first, because although the lam used in mental covenant is not literal, according to the verification, the relation in it is likening a non-specific one to a specific one, so it enters into metaphor. And the last four are not among the relations of linguistic metaphor mentioned here, although metaphor by deletion may be applied to what has a thing estimated and another placed in its place in i'rab, and metaphor by addition to what is added. Then counting deletion of mudaf and mudaf ilayhi separately, despite their inclusion in deletion absolutely, is not commendable.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and critical discussion of types of deletion and addition in metaphor. The author first lists four numbered types: deletion of the mudaf, deletion of the mudaf ilayhi, absolute deletion, and addition. He then critiques the counting of the last four (actually the last four types? The phrase 'الخمسة الأخيرة' is ambiguous but likely refers to the last five of a larger set) as being discussed separately. The first type (which is the 'al-dhikri? Actually it says 'الأول' meaning the first of the five? It's a bit confusing but the author explains that the lam used in 'ahd dhihni' is not literal but is a metaphor through likening. Then he states that the last four are not from the relations of linguistic metaphor as defined here, though some may apply 'majaz bi al-hadhf' and 'majaz bi al-ziyada' in other contexts. He concludes that counting mudaf and mudaf ilayhi deletions separately, when they fall under absolute deletion, is not commendable. This shows the author's care in avoiding redundancy in logical classification.

Classification of Concepts: Universal vs Particular
Author defines the division of concepts based on intellectual allowance of multiplicity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any concept as universal or particular based on the intellect's allowance of multiplicity.

Core Idea
Universality is a matter of intellectual possibility, not actual existence.

This passage from the logic text defines how concepts are divided into universal (كلي) and particular (جزئي). The criterion is whether the intellect, purely from the concept itself, can conceive multiple individuals under it. This distinction is fundamental for definitions and syllogisms in Islamic logic, and it directly applies to theological categories like the Necessary Being and possible beings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the division criterion
The division is based on whether the intellect (العقل) allows (جوّز) multiplicity (تكثر) from the perspective of its conception (من حيث تصوره). This is a purely mental possibility, not dependent on actual existence.
2
Recognize the two main branches
If multiplicity is allowed, the concept is universal (كلي); if not, it is particular (جزئي).
3
Explore the subcategories of universals
The author lists three types of universals based on the status of their individuals: (a) impossible individuals (ممتنع أفراده), like hypothetical universals (كليات فرضية) such as 'nothingness'; (b) possible but non-existent individuals, like the phoenix (عنقاء); (c) existent individuals – either in one person (like the Necessary Being, الواجب) or in many (like possible beings, الممكن).
Note that even a concept with only one actual individual (e.g., God) is still universal because the intellect can conceivably apply it to more than one.
4
Apply the criterion to examples
Test each example: 'nothingness' allows the intellect to conceive of multiple instances (though impossible in reality), so it is universal. 'Phoenix' allows multiple (though nonexistent). 'Necessary Being' allows multiple in conception (even if only one exists). 'Human' allows many. Conversely, 'Zayd' (as a concept of a specific person) does not allow multiplicity – it is particular.
5
Distinguish between actual existence and intellectual possibility
Remember that universality is about what the mind can conceive, not what exists. A universal concept may have zero, one, or many actual individuals; what matters is the intellectual allowance of multiplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المفهوم إن جوز العقل تكثره من حيث تصوره فكلي ممتنع أفراده كالكليات الفرضية أو لا يمتنع إما معدوم كالعنقاء أو موجود في شخص أو أكثر كالواجب والممكن وإلا يجوز تكثره من حيث نفس تصوره فجزئي
The concept: if the intellect allows its multiplicity in terms of its conception, then it is universal, [either] its individuals are impossible, like hypothetical universals, or not impossible – either non-existent like the phoenix, or existent in one person or more, like the Necessary and the possible. And if it does not allow its multiplicity in terms of its very conception, then it is particular.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage is a definitional classification of concepts (المفاهيم) into two types: universal (كلي) and particular (جزئي). The criterion is whether the intellect can conceive multiple individuals that fall under that concept, purely from its own form (من حيث تصوره), without considering external factors. If the intellect can conceive multiple individuals, the concept is universal. The author then subdivides the universal into three kinds: (1) those whose individuals are impossible, such as 'nothingness' or hypothetical constructs (كليات فرضية) that have no actual existence; (2) those that are possible but currently non-existent, like the phoenix (عنقاء); and (3) those that are existent, either in a single individual (like the Necessary Being, God) or in multiple individuals (like possible beings). On the other hand, if the intellect cannot conceive multiple individuals under the concept, it is particular. This division is foundational for understanding how terms refer to individuals in logic, especially in definitions and syllogisms. The examples are drawn from theological and metaphysical contexts: the Necessary Being (واجب) and possible beings (ممكن) are key categories in Islamic philosophy. The classification mirrors the Aristotelian division of universals but is adapted to the Islamic framework, distinguishing between what can and cannot have instances.

Key Terms
المفهوم
كلي
جزئي
كليات فرضية

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The concept of 'human'

Key Takeaway
A concept is universal if the intellect can conceive of multiple individuals under it, regardless of whether those individuals actually exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Defining the Particular in Islamic Logic
Author defines particular and cites abstraction levels from al-Tusi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a concept is a particular (juz'i) or a universal (kulli) using the criterion of simultaneous multiplicity in the mind.

Core Idea
A particular is what the mind cannot conceive as multiple at the same time.

Before we dive into scattered individuals, we need a firm grasp of what a 'particular' is in Mantiq. The author, citing al-Nasir al-Tusi, explains three levels of abstraction—sensory, imaginative, intellectual—that clarify how particulars are known. This lesson anchors the definition of the particular and its contrast with the universal.

Guided Path
1
Find the criterion for particularity
The author states that the mind cannot allow multiplication of some things at all, like Zayd. This is the defining feature of a particular.
The opposite is the universal, which the mind can apply to many.
2
Identify the three abstraction levels
Al-Tusi describes sensory, imaginative, and intellectual abstraction. Sensory forms depend on matter, imaginative forms are freer, intellectual forms are completely abstract.
This shows how particulars are known in the mind.
3
Apply to an example from Islamic sciences
Consider 'this specific Qur'an' vs. 'the concept of a book'. The former is a particular because you cannot have two identical copies simultaneously in the mind? Actually two copies can exist externally, but the particular is the individual copy, not the concept.
The key is mental conception, not external existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في المعاني المفردة حيث إنها معروضة للعوارض الحسية علم، ومع قطع النظر عنها معلوم ومعروض للجزئية، والشخص الخارجي معلوم بالعرض. ولما كان التعين الذهني المميز التام مغايرا للتعين الخارجي حكم بأن شخص الحسي شبح للخارجي قال النصير الطوسي: فالصور المحسوسة منتزعة انتزاعا ناقصا مشروطا بحضور المادة، والخيالية منتزعة انتزاعا أكثر، لكنه غير تام والعقلية منتزعة انتزاعا تاما هذا غاية التقرير من قبلهم، والعلم الحق عند علام الغيوب. ثم لما كان من أقسام الجزئي ما لا يُجَوِّز العقل تكثره أصلا، كزيد مثلا، ومنها الفرد المنتشر وهو يطلق على معنيين: الأول ما كان معينا غير قابل للشركة في الواقع، إلا أن العقل اشتبه عليه، فيحكم عليه تارة بأنه هذا أم ذلك على البدل، والثاني ما يكون في الواقع قابلا للشركة على البدل، وكان يتوهم انتقاض تعريف الكلي منعا والجزئي جمعا أراد أن يشير إلى دفعه، وقال: (فَمَحْسُوسُ الطِّفْلِ في مَبْدَ الْوِلَادَةِ الذي هو فرد منتشر بالمعنى الثاني، وَشَيْخِ ضَعِيفِ الْبَصَرِ، وَالصُّورَةِ الْخَيَالِيَّةِ مِنَ الْبَيْضَةِ الْمُعَيَّنَةِ الذين هما فردان منتشران بالمعنى الأول كُلُّهَا جُزْئِيَّاتٌ، لأَنَّ شَيْئًا مِنْهَا لَا يُجَوِّزُ الْعَقْلُ تَكَثُرَهُ عَلَى سَبِيلِ الِاجْتِمَاعِ)، بأن يجوز العقل في وقت واحد أنه هذا وهذا. وَهُوَ الْمُرَادُ في تعريف الكلي والجزئي، وقال بعض الشراح: لا أكاد أفقه واقعية الفرد المنتشر بالمعنى الثاني؛ لأن الانتشار لا يكون إلا بتقيد الكلي، والتقيد إما كلي فلا يفيد الجزئية، أو جزئي غير قابل للاشتراك، فيكون المقيد أيضا كذلك، أو قابل له على البدل، وهو منتشر، وينجر الكلام في انتشاره، ولا يخفى فساده؛ لأن الفرد المنتشر عبارة عن الكلي المعروض لشخص خاص يمتنع اشتراكه اجتماعا لا بدلا كما أن الجزئي الغير المنتشر عبارة عنه، من حيث هو معروض الهذية المانعة عنه بالكلية، والتشخص أمر عدمي، والكلي غير مقيد به، ولو سلم أنه كلي مقيد فنقول: إنه مقيد بتشخص هو منتشر بنفسه، كما أن التشخص المانع من الشركة بالكلية متعين بنفسه عند من يراه أمرا موجودا. نعم، يرد على الفرد المنتشر أن وجوده في جميع الأشخاص يوجب الشركة على الاجتماع وعليها مدار الكلية ووجوده في البعض دون بعض ترجيح من غير مرجح، فإذن الحق أن لا وجود له في الأعيان، وإنما يلاحظ بواسطة الحاسة أمر لو وجد في الأعيان لكان عين واحد من الأفراد على البدل، فإن كان مقصودهم هذا فلا غبار عليه، وإلا فغير محصل.
A chapter on simple concepts. Since they are subject to sensory accidents, they are known; and when disregarding them, they are known [as they are] and subject to particularity; the external individual is known incidentally. Since the mental determination that perfectly distinguishes is different from external determination, it is judged that the sensory individual is a phantom of the external. al-Nasir al-Tusi said: 'Sensory forms are abstracted deficiently, conditioned by the presence of matter; imaginative forms are abstracted more but not completely; intellectual forms are abstracted completely.' This is the extent of their report; true knowledge is with the Knower of the Unseen. Then, since among the divisions of the particular is that which the mind does not allow to be multiplied at all, like Zayd for example, and among them is the scattered individual, which is used in two senses: first, what is determinate but not actually capable of sharing, except that the mind is confused about it, so it judges it sometimes as this or that alternatively; second, what is actually capable of sharing alternatively; and [the author] intended to point out the refutation of the suspicion that the definition of the universal is violated by prevention and the particular by collection, and he said: 'The child's sensation at the beginning of birth, which is a scattered individual in the second sense, and the weak-sighted elder, and the imaginative image of a specific egg, which are two scattered individuals in the first sense, all are particulars, because nothing in them does the mind allow its multiplication simultaneously,' meaning that the mind cannot judge at one time that it is this and this. And that is what is meant in the definition of the universal and the particular. Some commentators said: 'I scarcely understand the reality of the scattered individual in the second sense, because scattering only occurs by restriction of the universal; the restriction is either universal, so it does not yield particularity, or particular not capable of sharing, so the restricted is also like that, or capable of sharing alternatively, which is scattered, and the discussion proceeds into its scattering.' Its corruption is not hidden, because the scattered individual is the universal presented to a specific individual whose sharing is impossible simultaneously, not alternatively, just as the non-scattered particular is it, by virtue of being presented with the form that prevents it entirely, and individualization is a non-entity, and the universal is not restricted by it; and if it is granted that it is a restricted universal, we say: it is restricted by an individualization that is itself scattered, just as the individualization that prevents sharing entirely is itself determinate for those who see it as an existent. Moreover, an objection against the scattered individual is that its existence in all individuals would necessitate sharing simultaneously, and upon it depends universality; and its existence in some individuals without others is a preference without a preferrer. So the truth is that it has no existence in external entities; rather, what is observed via sense is something that, if it existed in external entities, would be one specific individual alternatively. If this is what they intend, then no objection; otherwise, it is not precise.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and analytical discussion of the nature of particulars, especially the concept of the 'scattered individual' (fard muntashir). The author begins by noting that simple concepts are subject to sensory accidents, but when considered apart from those accidents, they are known in themselves and are the subjects of particularity. External individuals are only known incidentally. Then, citing al-Nasir al-Tusi, the author describes three degrees of abstraction: sensory forms are abstracted deficiently, requiring the presence of matter; imaginative forms are abstracted more; and intellectual forms are abstracted completely. This sets the background for understanding how particulars exist in the mind. Next, the author divides particular individuals: some, like Zayd, cannot be conceived as multiple at all. Another type is the 'scattered individual' (fard muntashir), which has two meanings: 1) an individual that is really determinate but the mind is uncertain whether it is this or that alternatively (e.g., the image of a specific egg that one cannot tell is one egg or another); 2) something actually capable of being shared among individuals on an alternating basis (e.g., a child's sensation at birth, which could be any child). The author then explains that the commentator intends to refute the suspicion that these examples break the definitions of universal (by prevention, i.e., a universal cannot be prevented from being shared) or particular (by collection, i.e., a particular cannot be collected with others). All examples are particulars because the mind cannot conceive them simultaneously. Then a critic objects that the second meaning of scattered individual is incoherent: scattering requires restriction of a universal, but that restriction either yields another universal (so no particularity), or a particular that cannot be shared (contradicts scattering), or a particular sharable alternatively (which just restates the problem). The author refutes this by explaining that the scattered individual is a universal presented to a specific individual, and individualization is a non-entity; the universal is not restricted by it. Finally, the author argues that the scattered individual has no real external existence because if it existed in all individuals it would be a universal, and if only in some, it would be arbitrary. Thus it is merely observed via sense as something that, if external, would be one specific individual. This concludes the author's analysis.

جزئي
كلي
تشخص

The Scattered Individual: Two Meanings
Author defines scattered individual and its two senses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two senses of 'scattered individual' (fard muntashir) and explain why each is still a particular.

Core Idea
A scattered individual is a particular that can be shared alternately, not simultaneously.

The concept of a 'scattered individual' might seem paradoxical: how can an individual be shared? The author explains that there are two meanings: one where the mind is confused about identity, and another where reality itself allows alternate sharing. This lesson breaks down these meanings and their examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two meanings in the text
Read the passage and locate the two definitions: 1) something determinate but the mind is confused (e.g., weak-sighted elder's perception); 2) something actually shareable on an alternating basis (e.g., child's sensation at birth).
Both are called 'scattered' because they cannot be conceived as multiple simultaneously.
2
Compare with the non-scattered particular
Zayd is a non-scattered particular: his essence prevents any sharing. Contrast this with the scattered individual which allows alternate sharing.
The key difference is that the scattered individual permits sharing 'on badal' (alternatively).
3
Apply the definition to the examples given
The author lists: the child's sensation (2nd sense), the weak-sighted elder's perception (1st sense), and the imaginative image of a specific egg (1st sense). Check each against the definition to see why they are particulars.
The mind cannot conceive these as multiple at the same time, even though they might be confused or shareable alternately.

البدل، وهو منتشر، وينجر الكلام في انتشاره، ولا يخفى فساده؛ لأن الفرد المنتشر عبارة عن الكلي المعروض لشخص خاص يمتنع اشتراكه اجتماعا لا بدلا كما أن الجزئي الغير المنتشر عبارة عنه، من حيث هو معروض الهذية المانعة عنه بالكلية، والتشخص أمر عدمي، والكلي غير مقيد به، ولو سلم أنه كلي مقيد فنقول: إنه مقيد بتشخص هو منتشر بنفسه، كما أن التشخص المانع من الشركة بالكلية متعين بنفسه عند من يراه أمرا موجودا. نعم، يرد على الفرد المنتشر أن وجوده في جميع الأشخاص يوجب الشركة على الاجتماع وعليها مدار الكلية ووجوده في البعض دون بعض ترجيح من غير مرجح، فإذن الحق أن لا وجود له في الأعيان، وإنما يلاحظ بواسطة الحاسة أمر لو وجد في الأعيان لكان عين واحد من الأفراد على البدل، فإن كان مقصودهم هذا فلا غبار عليه، وإلا فغير محصل.
A chapter on simple concepts. Since they are subject to sensory accidents, they are known; and when disregarding them, they are known [as they are] and subject to particularity; the external individual is known incidentally. Since the mental determination that perfectly distinguishes is different from external determination, it is judged that the sensory individual is a phantom of the external. al-Nasir al-Tusi said: 'Sensory forms are abstracted deficiently, conditioned by the presence of matter; imaginative forms are abstracted more but not completely; intellectual forms are abstracted completely.' This is the extent of their report; true knowledge is with the Knower of the Unseen. Then, since among the divisions of the particular is that which the mind does not allow to be multiplied at all, like Zayd for example, and among them is the scattered individual, which is used in two senses: first, what is determinate but not actually capable of sharing, except that the mind is confused about it, so it judges it sometimes as this or that alternatively; second, what is actually capable of sharing alternatively; and [the author] intended to point out the refutation of the suspicion that the definition of the universal is violated by prevention and the particular by collection, and he said: 'The child's sensation at the beginning of birth, which is a scattered individual in the second sense, and the weak-sighted elder, and the imaginative image of a specific egg, which are two scattered individuals in the first sense, all are particulars, because nothing in them does the mind allow its multiplication simultaneously,' meaning that the mind cannot judge at one time that it is this and this. And that is what is meant in the definition of the universal and the particular. Some commentators said: 'I scarcely understand the reality of the scattered individual in the second sense, because scattering only occurs by restriction of the universal; the restriction is either universal, so it does not yield particularity, or particular not capable of sharing, so the restricted is also like that, or capable of sharing alternatively, which is scattered, and the discussion proceeds into its scattering.' Its corruption is not hidden, because the scattered individual is the universal presented to a specific individual whose sharing is impossible simultaneously, not alternatively, just as the non-scattered particular is it, by virtue of being presented with the form that prevents it entirely, and individualization is a non-entity, and the universal is not restricted by it; and if it is granted that it is a restricted universal, we say: it is restricted by an individualization that is itself scattered, just as the individualization that prevents sharing entirely is itself determinate for those who see it as an existent. Moreover, an objection against the scattered individual is that its existence in all individuals would necessitate sharing simultaneously, and upon it depends universality; and its existence in some individuals without others is a preference without a preferrer. So the truth is that it has no existence in external entities; rather, what is observed via sense is something that, if it existed in external entities, would be one specific individual alternatively. If this is what they intend, then no objection; otherwise, it is not precise.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and analytical discussion of the nature of particulars, especially the concept of the 'scattered individual' (fard muntashir). The author begins by noting that simple concepts are subject to sensory accidents, but when considered apart from those accidents, they are known in themselves and are the subjects of particularity. External individuals are only known incidentally. Then, citing al-Nasir al-Tusi, the author describes three degrees of abstraction: sensory forms are abstracted deficiently, requiring the presence of matter; imaginative forms are abstracted more; and intellectual forms are abstracted completely. This sets the background for understanding how particulars exist in the mind. Next, the author divides particular individuals: some, like Zayd, cannot be conceived as multiple at all. Another type is the 'scattered individual' (fard muntashir), which has two meanings: 1) an individual that is really determinate but the mind is uncertain whether it is this or that alternatively (e.g., the image of a specific egg that one cannot tell is one egg or another); 2) something actually capable of being shared among individuals on an alternating basis (e.g., a child's sensation at birth, which could be any child). The author then explains that the commentator intends to refute the suspicion that these examples break the definitions of universal (by prevention, i.e., a universal cannot be prevented from being shared) or particular (by collection, i.e., a particular cannot be collected with others). All examples are particulars because the mind cannot conceive them simultaneously. Then a critic objects that the second meaning of scattered individual is incoherent: scattering requires restriction of a universal, but that restriction either yields another universal (so no particularity), or a particular that cannot be shared (contradicts scattering), or a particular sharable alternatively (which just restates the problem). The author refutes this by explaining that the scattered individual is a universal presented to a specific individual, and individualization is a non-entity; the universal is not restricted by it. Finally, the author argues that the scattered individual has no real external existence because if it existed in all individuals it would be a universal, and if only in some, it would be arbitrary. Thus it is merely observed via sense as something that, if external, would be one specific individual. This concludes the author's analysis.

The scattered individual is a particular that allows alternate sharing, and its two senses depend on whether the source of scattering is mental confusion or real possibility.

Defending the Scattered Individual: Objection and Response
Author presents and refutes a criticism of the scattered individual concept.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the logical objection against the second meaning of scattered individual and explain the author's defense that it is a mental construct with no real external existence.

Core Idea
The scattered individual exists only in the mind, not externally, and this resolves the logical difficulties.

Some commentators find the second meaning of scattered individual incoherent: if it is restricted by a universal, it remains a universal; if restricted by a particular, it cannot be scattered. The author responds with a subtle analysis: the scattered individual is a universal presented to a specific individual, and individualization is a non-entity. He further argues it has no external existence, only a mental one. This lesson walks through the objection, defense, and final position.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
A commentator argues that the second meaning is incoherent: scattering requires restriction of a universal, but the restriction either yields a universal (no particularity) or a non-shareable particular (no scattering).
The objection aims to show that the concept of a scattered individual is logically flawed.
2
Follow the author's refutation
The author counters that the scattered individual is a universal presented to a specific individual, and individualization (tashakhkhus) is a non-entity, so the universal is not truly restricted. He also notes that if one insists it is a restricted universal, the restriction itself is scattered.
The author uses the idea that individualization is negative to dissolve the problem.
3
Analyze the final position
The author then argues that the scattered individual cannot exist externally: if it existed in all individuals it would be universal; if only in some, it would be arbitrary. Therefore, it only exists as a mental observation of something that would be a single individual if it were external.
This concludes the defense: the scattered individual is a mental construct with no external reality.

البدل، وهو منتشر، وينجر الكلام في انتشاره، ولا يخفى فساده؛ لأن الفرد المنتشر عبارة عن الكلي المعروض لشخص خاص يمتنع اشتراكه اجتماعا لا بدلا كما أن الجزئي الغير المنتشر عبارة عنه، من حيث هو معروض الهذية The True Particular and Its Predicability
Author defines the true particular and corrects al-Sayyid's view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-juz'ī al-ḥaqīqī and explain why it can be predicated of mental existence, correcting a common misconception.

Core Idea
The true particular shares identity between external and mental, and can be predicated—just not of another individual.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies the nature of the 'true particular' (al-juz'ī al-ḥaqīqī). Some earlier logicians, like al-Sayyid al-Sharif, argued that particulars are never predicated. The commentator corrects this by showing that the particular, defined as the identity common between an external person and its mental representation, can indeed be predicated—but only of a mental existence, not of another external particular. This lesson unpacks that definition and the correction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find the phrase 'الجزئي الحقيقي' in the Arabic. Notice that it is defined as 'الهوية المشتركة بين الشخص الخارجي والذهني' (the identity common between the external person and the mental person). This is the definiendum.
The definition includes both external and mental aspects.
2
Extract the definition's components
The definition has two parts: (1) it is a common identity (هوية مشتركة), and (2) it is predicated (محمولا). The commentator emphasizes that the particular is indeed predicated, contrary to al-Sayyid's claim.
The predicability is limited: it applies to mental existence, not to another external individual.
3
Analyze the correction of al-Sayyid
The commentator dismisses the objection that particulars are not predicated at all, suggesting al-Sayyid may have meant that an individual person is not predicated of its like. The commentator defers a full response to a later discussion.
This shows the precision required in logical definitions: a particular concept (e.g., 'Zayd') can be said of the mental image of Zayd.
4
Apply the definition to an Islamic science example
Consider the term 'Zayd' in a ruling: 'Zayd is fasting'. In the external world, Zayd is a unique individual, so the term 'Zayd' cannot be predicated of another person. But in the mind, the concept 'Zayd' can be applied to the mental image of Zayd. This distinction is crucial in usul al-fiqh when analyzing the subject of a ruling.
This illustrates why the definition matters: it determines the scope of predication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في المعاني المفردة
كون الجزئي الحقيقي، وهو الهوية المشتركة بين الشخص الخارجي والذهني (محمولا)، لا كما زعم السيد قدس سره: أن الجزئي لا يحمل على شيء أصلا، ولعل مراده قدس سره أن الشخص لا يحمل على مثله. سيجيئ فانتظره. (وهو) أي كون الجزئي محمولا (الحق)
ولا يجاب، كما أجاب السيد المحقق قدس سره (با)
في تعريف الكلي (صدقها) أي صدق الصورة (على الكثيرين هو) أي الكلي (ظل لها، أي للكثيرين، ومنتزع عنها): فإن الكلي ينتزع عن الجزئيات، (واللازم ههنا أن لها، أي للصورة الخارجية، كزيد مثلا (ظلاً متعددا، لا أنها ظل متعدد، بل ظل واحد، (والمطلب الثاني). ولم يتحقق في مورد النقض، ([ وذلك ] لأن الصورة الخارجية والصورة الذهنية متصادقة، والتصادق يصحح الانتزاع)، أي انتزاع كل منهما عن الأخرى، (والظلية) أي ظلية كل منهما (أيضا: فإن الموجب للانتزاع الاتحاد من الطرفين).
Chapter on Simple Meanings: That the true particular, which is the identity common between the external person and the mental [person], is predicated, not as al-Sayyid claimed: that the particular is not predicated of anything at all. Perhaps his intention is that the person is not predicated of its like. It will come later, so wait for it. (And it) i.e., the particular being predicated (is the truth). And it is not answered, as the verifier al-Sayyid answered (with)... In the definition of the universal: (its truth) i.e., the truth of the form (upon the many it is) i.e., the universal (a shadow of them, i.e., of the many, and derived from them): for the universal is derived from the particulars. (And the necessary here is that it has, i.e., the external form, like Zayd for example, multiple shadows, not that it is multiple shadows, but one shadow. (And the second demand). And it was not verified in the locus of the objection. ([And that] because the external form and the mental form are co-extensive, and co-extensiveness validates derivation) i.e., derivation of each from the other. (And the shadow-ness) i.e., shadow-ness of each (also: for that which necessitates derivation is union from both sides).
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage from Bahr Ulum, a commentary on Sullam al-Ulum, defines key terms in logic: the particular (جزئي) and the universal (كلي). The author begins by stating that the true particular (الجزئي الحقيقي) — the identity shared between an external individual (like Zayd) and its mental representation — can be predicated (محمول). This corrects a view attributed to al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjani, who claimed the particular is never predicated. The commentator suggests al-Sayyid may have meant that an individual person is not predicated of another individual, not that the particular concept is never predicated. The author then defines the universal as the truth of a form (like the concept of 'human') that applies to many individuals; it is a 'shadow' (ظل) of those individuals, derived from them. For example, the external form of Zayd has multiple shadows because it corresponds to many mental images, but it does not become multiple shadows itself. The second demand (المطلب الثاني) is that the external form and mental form are co-extensive (متصادقة), meaning they apply to each other, which allows derivation (انتزاع) of each from the other. The shadow-ness (الظلية) of each is also derived from the union (اتحاد) between the two sides — the external and mental existences. This establishes that the universal is a shadow of its particulars, both in external and mental realms.

Key Terms
الجزئي الحقيقي
الهوية المشتركة
الشخص الخارجي
الشخص الذهني

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It is a shared identity between an external person and its mental representation, and can be applied to the mental one.
C) It is a universal that applies to many individuals.
D) Al-Sayyid al-Sharif argued that it is predicated of every external individual.

Key Takeaway
The true particular can be predicated of mental existence, correcting the misconception that particulars are never predicated.

Use the distinction from the passage.

The Universal as Shadow: Derivation and Co-extensiveness
Author defines the universal as a shadow derived from particulars via co-extensiveness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the metaphor of the universal as a 'shadow of the many' and the condition of co-extensiveness required for derivation.

Core Idea
The universal is a shadow of its particulars, derived through co-extensiveness between external and mental forms.

The commentator introduces a distinctive metaphor: the universal is a 'shadow' (ظل) of its particulars. This means the universal form, though applying to many individuals, has a derivative existence compared to the concrete particulars. The passage also explains that for a universal to be derived from particulars, the external and mental forms must be co-extensive (متصادقة), meaning they apply to each other. This lesson unpacks the shadow metaphor and the logical condition for abstraction.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of the universal
Find the phrase 'صدقها على الكثيرين هو ظل لها' in the Arabic. The commentator defines the universal as 'the truth of the form upon the many', which he then calls a 'shadow of them'.
The form mentioned is the mental representation (صورة) that applies to multiple individuals.
2
Parse the shadow metaphor
The commentator says the universal is a shadow of the many. A shadow is derivative, not primary. So the universal is secondary to its particulars. For example, the concept 'human' is derived from individual humans like Zayd and 'Amr.
This metaphor is unique to the Bahr Ulum tradition and emphasizes the ontological dependence of universals.
3
Understand the condition of co-extensiveness
The passage states that the external form (e.g., the actual Zayd) and the mental form (the concept of Zayd) are co-extensive (متصادقة). This means they apply to each other: the concept of Zayd applies to the external Zayd, and the external Zayd corresponds to the mental image. This co-extensiveness validates the derivation (انتزاع) of each from the other.
Without co-extensiveness, the universal would not be a true shadow of the particulars.
4
Apply the concept to an Islamic science example
In usul al-fiqh, the universal 'wine' is derived from particular instances (e.g., this cup of wine, that bottle). The prohibition of wine applies to all instances. The co-extensiveness between the universal concept and the external instances is what allows the ruling to be extended. If the universal were not a true shadow (i.e., not derived from all relevant particulars), the ruling might miss some cases.
This shows the practical importance of correct derivation in legal reasoning.

Chapter on Simple Meanings: That the true particular, which is the identity common between the external person and the mental [person], is predicated, not as al-Sayyid claimed: that the particular is not predicated of anything at all. Perhaps his intention is that the person is not predicated of its like. It will come later, so wait for it. (And it) i.e., the particular being predicated (is the truth). And it is not answered, as the verifier al-Sayyid answered (with)... In the definition of the universal: (its truth) i.e., the truth of the form (upon the many it is) i.e., the universal (a shadow of them, i.e., of the many, and derived from them): for the universal is derived from the particulars. (And the necessary here is that it has, i.e., the external form, like Zayd for example, multiple shadows, not that it is multiple shadows, but one shadow. (And the second demand). Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage from Bahr Ulum, a commentary on Sullam al-Ulum, defines key terms in logic: the particular (جزئي) and the universal (كلي). The author begins by stating that the true particular (الجزئي الحقيقي) — the identity shared between an external individual (like Zayd) and its mental representation — can be predicated (محمول). This corrects a view attributed to al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjani, who claimed the particular is never predicated. The commentator suggests al-Sayyid may have meant that an individual person is not predicated of another individual, not that the particular concept is never predicated. The author then defines the universal as the truth of a form (like the concept of 'human') that applies to many individuals; it is a 'shadow' (ظل) of those individuals, derived from them. For example, the external form of Zayd has multiple shadows because it corresponds to many mental images, but it does not become multiple shadows itself. The second demand (المطلب الثاني) is that the external form and mental form are co-extensive (متصادقة), meaning they apply to each other, which allows derivation (انتزاع) of each from the other. The shadow-ness (الظلية) of each is also derived from the union (اتحاد) between the two sides — the external and mental existences. This establishes that the universal is a shadow of its particulars, both in external and mental realms.

Universals as Mental Shadows: The Levels of Existence
Author critiques the shadow analogy and establishes three levels of existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three levels of existence for a thing (external, sensory, intellectual) and explain why the universal is a mental attribute, not an external entity.

Core Idea
Universals are not external realities but mental shadows abstracted from individuals.

This passage from Bahr al-Ulum's commentary on Sullam al-Ulum critiques earlier views and establishes a nuanced ontology of universals. You will learn why correspondence (tasadiq) does not entail abstraction, and how the shadow analogy is refined into a three-level schema of existence. The lesson will equip you to distinguish between external, sensory, and intellectual existence and to recognize that universality and particularity are attributes of the known, not of external objects.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial objection
Locate the claim that correspondence (tasadiq) does not necessitate abstraction (intiza'). The example 'human' and 'writer' both apply to one person, but neither is abstracted from the other. This shows that mere co-application does not force a relation of derivation.
2
Refute the shadow analogy as originally stated
The analogy that external form relates to imaginary form as man relates to his individuals is rejected because a fully determinate thing cannot be determined again to produce a shadow. Determinateness is unique and cannot be replicated through a shadow.
The author allows a refined version where external form is taken as a form abstracted to a degree.
3
Extract the three levels of existence
The author identifies three levels: (1) external existence (primary, has effects), (2) sensory existence (shadow related to one individual – sensory form), (3) intellectual existence (shadow related to many – intellectual form). The first is particular, the second is also particular (sensory form of a single thing), the third is universal.
Universality and particularity are attributes of knowledge, not of external existence.
4
Understand the precise view of the universal
After removing mental individuators, what remains is a mental concept that applies to many. The universal is that concept – a shadow of the many, existing only in the mind. Thus universality is an attribute of the known, not an extramental entity.
This aligns with the commentary on al-Talwihat and is the sound interpretation.

Source Text
