الاتِّحَادَ مِنَ الطَّرَفَيْن). شرح بحر العلوم على سلم العلوم. قال بعض محققي شراح هذا الكتاب إن التصادق لا يوجب الانتزاع، ولیس مداره على الاتحاد مطلقا، وكيف يجوز العاقل أن الإنسان منتزع من الكاتب ، بل المتأصل في الوجود يكون منتزعا منه ، وغير المتأصل منتزعا. وهذا سؤال حق على تقرير المصنف، قال البعض الآخر من الشراح: إن نسبة الصورة الخارجية إلى الصورة الخيالية كنسبة الإنسان إلى أشخاصه، فيكون ظلا ،منتزعا، كما أنه منتزع وظل ولا يخفى ما فيه؛ فإن المتعين التام لا يمكن تعينه بتعين آخر، حتى يكون نسبتها إليها كنسبة الإنسان إلى أشخاصه، إلا أن يراد بالصورة الخارجية الصورة المجردة تجريدا ما، وهي الهوية التي يمكن وجودها في الحاسة، واكتنافها بالعوارض الخيالية، ووجودها في الخارج واكتنافها بالعوارض الخارجية ، كما ذكرنا مشروحا. وتحقيق المقام أن للشيء وجودا خارج المشاعر، وهو يترتب عليه الآثار الخارجية، وهو أصل في الوجود، ووجود فيها لا يترتب عليها تلك ، وهو ظل للأول، ولبعض الموجودات الظلية مناسبة بواحد شخصي، ولذلك يكون إدراكا له ، وهو صورته الحسية، فهي ظل له ، ولبعضها بالكثير، فهو ظل لها، وهو الصورة العقلية، والقسم الأول يسمى جزئيا والثاني كليا، فعلى هذا هما من صفات العلم. الآثار ، هذا بحسب الجلي من النظر ، ثم النظر الدقيق يحكم أن ظل الكثير حقيقة ليس إلا ما يبقى بعد حذف المشخصات الذهنية، موجودا في الذهن؛ إذ هو المنطبق على الكثير، والمنتزع منها المتحد معها، لكن لما لم يكن وجودها إلا في ضمن الشخص الذهني حكم بأنه ظل، فإذن الكلي عبارة عن ذلك المفهوم، وقس عليه حال الجزئية، فصارت الكلية والجزئية من المعلوم، وإلى هذا يشير شرح التلويحات أيضا، وهذا محمل صحيح السيد المحقق قدس سره الشريف .
Union from both sides. Commentary of Bahr al-Ulum on Sullam al-Ulum. Some of the verified commentators of this book said: 'Correspondence does not necessitate abstraction, nor is its criterion absolute union. How can a rational person allow that 'human' is abstracted from 'writer'? Rather, that which is primary in existence is that from which it is abstracted, and that which is non-primary is that which is abstracted.' This is a valid objection according to the author's formulation. Another group of commentators said: 'The relation of the external form to the imaginary form is like the relation of man to his individuals, so it is a shadow and abstracted, just as it is abstracted and a shadow.' But it is not hidden what is in it; for the fully determinate cannot be determined by another determination, such that its relation to them is like the relation of man to his individuals, unless by the external form is meant the form abstracted with some abstraction, which is the identity that can exist in the sense, accompanied by imaginary accidents, and its existence in the external accompanied by external accidents, as we mentioned in detail. And the verification of the station is that a thing has an existence outside the senses, and it entails external effects, and it is primary in existence, and an existence in them (the senses) does not entail those effects, and it is a shadow of the first. Some shadowy existences have a relation to a single individual, so they are an apprehension of it, and it is its sensory form, so it is a shadow of it. Some of them have a relation to many, so it is a shadow of them, and it is the intellectual form. The first division is called particular and the second universal; thus they are attributes of knowledge. Effects. This is according to the apparent view. Then the precise view judges that the shadow of the many is nothing but what remains after the removal of mental individuators, existing in the mind, for it is that which applies to the many, abstracted from them, united with them. But since its existence is only within the mental individual, it is judged to be a shadow. So the universal is that concept, and analogize the case of particularity. Thus universality and particularity become attributes of the known. This is indicated by the commentary of al-Talwihat as well, and this is a sound interpretation of the words of the master, the verifier, may his noble secret be sanctified.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical and definitional discussion on the nature of universals and particulars. It opens with an objection: correspondence (tasadiq) does not entail abstraction (intiza'), as in 'human' and 'writer' where one is not abstracted from the other. The author concedes this is valid. Then another group proposes that the external form relates to the imaginary form like man to his individuals, making the imaginary form a shadow abstracted from the external. The author refutes this by noting that a fully determinate thing cannot be determined again. Instead, the external form is understood as a form abstracted to a degree—an identity with sensory and imaginary accidents. The author then gives his own verification: a thing has three levels of existence: external (primary, has effects), sensory (shadow related to one individual), and intellectual (shadow related to many). The first is particular, the second universal—both are attributes of knowledge, not external existence. A more precise analysis holds that the universal (shadow of many) is what remains in the mind after stripping mental individuators, applying to many. Thus universality and particularity are attributes of the known, as supported by the commentary on al-Talwihat. This lesson guards against mistaking universals for real external entities.

Why Universals Do Not Multiply Externally: The Role of Hadiyya
Clarifies the universal's external multiplication and role of hadiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between sensory forms (with hadiyya) and intellectual forms (without hadiyya) and explain why hypothetical universals are true universals.

Core Idea
Hadiyya blocks external multiplicity; its absence enables universality.

We often speak of universals as concepts that apply to many things. But if a concept existed in the external world, would it be one thing or many? The author resolves this by introducing 'hadiyya' (identity), the factor that makes a form particular. Sensory forms have hadiyya, so they cannot multiply externally; intellectual forms lack it, so they can be shared. This distinction is crucial for understanding the nature of universals, especially hypothetical universals (kulliyat faradiyya) and secondary intelligibles (ma'qulat thaniya), which are true universals precisely because they have no hadiyya.

Guided Path
1
Identify the concept of hadiyya
Hadiyya is a unique characteristic that prevents a concept from being shared among multiple individuals. In the passage, the form of Zayd in different minds all share the same hadiyya, so they are all identical to Zayd himself—they cannot multiply externally.
Think of hadiyya as the 'thisness' that makes an individual unique.
2
Distinguish sensory forms from intellectual forms
Sensory forms (like the image of Zayd in your senses) have hadiyya—they are tied to one specific individual. Intellectual forms (like the concept of Zayd abstracted) lack hadiyya, so they can be shared among many minds.
The passage says: 'the sensible is only that which includes an identity that prevents sharing, so it is particular'.
3
Understand why hypothetical universals are universals
Hypothetical universals (like 'triangle') and secondary intelligibles (like 'universality') have no hadiyya at all. Therefore, the mind does not restrict them—they can apply to many instances externally. The only limitation comes from logical contradictions, not from identity.
This is why they are called 'hypothetical'—they are universal by nature, not tied to any specific existence.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Rather, the answer is that what is meant in the definition of the universal is 'the concept multiplied according to the external.' So the form obtained from Zayd with respect to intellects is impossible to multiply in the external; rather, all of them are the identity of Zayd. For it applies to each one of them, based on the verification that was indicated: that if it existed in the external, it would be identical to Zayd. And this is what is meant by 'the obtaining of things by their essences, not by their phantoms,' as in the hashia. Perhaps the secret in it is that you already know that the form taken in the senses has a relation only to one individual, and it has an identity by which the mind distinguishes it from others, unlike the form taken in the intellect. So it is true of the first class that if it existed in the external, it would be a specific single entity, unlike the second. And you already know that the sensible is only that which includes an identity that prevents sharing, so it is particular, the mind does not allow it to be shared, unlike the known of the intellectual form. So master this, and pay no attention to idle talk. As for the hypothetical universals and secondary intelligibles, because they lack identity, the mind, by merely conceptualizing them, refrains from allowing their multiplication in the external. Since you know that the preventing factor is sensory knowledge, and it only relates to that which has identity. So it is said: 'The hypothetical universals are with respect to the existing realities universals,' because the mind does not, by merely conceptualizing them, refrain from applying them, but only when it notices incompatibility between them, or establishes

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and explanatory continuation. It gives the definitive answer: the universal concept, such as the form of Zayd in multiple intellects, does not multiply in the external because all those forms are identical to Zayd's identity. The phrase 'obtaining things by their essences not by phantoms' indicates that the mental form of Zayd, if externalized, would be Zayd himself. The secret is that sensory forms have a unique identity (hadiyya) that makes them particular—each is tied to one individual. Intellectual forms lack such identity, so they can be shared. Thus the mind does not allow sensory forms to be predicated of many, but intellectual forms are predicable of many. Hypothetical universals (kulliyat faradiyya) and secondary intelligibles (ma'qulat thaniya) lack identity (hadiyya) entirely, so the mind immediately allows their external multiplication. The preventing factor is sensory knowledge, which only attaches to what has identity. Therefore, hypothetical universals are true universals with respect to existing realities, because the mind does not initially restrict them—only when contradiction is observed does it limit their application. This lesson guards against confusing the particularity of sensory forms with the universality of intellectual concepts.

The key to understanding universals is hadiyya: sensory forms have it and are particular; intellectual forms lack it and are universal; hypothetical universals lack it entirely, making them true universals.

Types of Universals: Possible vs. Impossible
Author defines two types of universals and their attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two types of universals (with possible non-existent individuals vs. with no individuals) and classify given concepts accordingly.

Core Idea
Not all universals have actual existent individuals; some are possible, others impossible.

This passage from Bahr al-Ulum's commentary on Sullam al-Ulum presents a crucial classification of universals. Understanding that some universals (like the phoenix) have possible but non-existent individuals, while others (like an impossible concept) have none, is key to grasping how universality relates to the known object rather than to knowledge itself. In this lesson, you will learn to apply this classification to examples from Islamic logic and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
The author states that universals are of two types: those that have possible non-existent individuals (أفراد ممكنة معدومة) and those that have no individuals at all (ما لا فرد له أصلاً). Locate these definitions in the Arabic.
The author calls the first type 'possible' (ممكن) because the individuals could exist, but currently don't; the second type is impossible (محال).
2
Examine the examples
The phoenix (العنقاء) is given as an example of the first type: it is a possible concept but has no actual individuals. The concept of 'impossible of both sides' (ضروري الطرفين) is an example of the second type: it can never have an individual. Note that the universality of the first type is 'with respect to' the concept, while the second type's universality is relative to both existent and non-existent realities.
The term 'necessary of both sides' refers to a square circle or other logical impossibilities.
3
Understand the attribute of universality
The author argues that universality and particularity are attributes of the known object (صفة المعلوم), not of knowledge. Mental particularity (التشخص الذهني) only distinguishes concepts in the mind, not from external particulars. The passage includes an objection and response regarding why sensory forms cannot be shared.
This step is more advanced; focus on the core claim that universality belongs to what is known, not to the act of knowing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلية والجزئية أم صفة
شرح بحر العلوم على سلم العلوم
البرهان. هذا ما تلقاه الجمهور من المحققين بالقبول، كالسيد المحقق قدس سره الشريف، والمحقق الدواني.
والأظهر أنها على ضربين منها ما له أفراد ممكنة معدومة، ويصدق عليها بعد فرض الوجود، كالعنقاء وسائر المفاهيم الممكنة المعدومة، وكليتها بالنظر إليها، ومنها ما لا فرد له أصلا، كمفهوم ضروري الطرفين وسائر المحالات، وكليتها بالنسبة إلى الحقائق الموجودة والمعدومة معا، (هذا)،
والله أعلم بالصواب.
( وَالْكُلّيّةُ وَالْجُزْئِيَّةُ صِفَةُ الْمَعْلُوم) ، كما بينا سابقا، (وَقِيلَ صِفَةُ الْعِلْم). صفة المعلوم، وتحقيق هذا القول : إن التشخص تابع لنحو الوجود عند أصحاب هذا القول، العلم فالتشخص الذي يحصل لمفهوم في الذهن إنما يميزه عن الصور الذهنية الأخر، لا عن الأشخاص الخارجية، بخلاف التعين الخارجي ؛ فإنه تابع للوجود الخارجي، فيميزه عن الأشخاص الخارجية الأخر، فالصورة العقلية المشخصة بالتشخص الذهني لا ينقبض العقل باعتبار نفس تصورها عن فرضها مشتركة في الأعيان، بمعنى أنها لو وجدت لكانت عين الأشخاص الخارجية من نوعه، بخلاف الصور الحسية والخيالية ؛ لاشتمالها على هوية مانعة عن التكثر في الخارج.
فإن قلت كما يجوز الشركة في الصور العقلية لم لا يجوز شركة الشخص الخارجي في الصور الحاصلة في الحاسة ، فيجوز أن يحصل من غير تجريد في الذهن ؟
قلت لو سلم جواز الشركة بحسب نفس التصور، فلا يلزم منه إمكان الحصول في نفس الأمر ؛ لعروض العوارض الخارجية المنافية للتشخص الذهني، هذا غاية التقرير لكلامهم، فإليه يشير ما في الحاشية: ذلك مذهب الأوائل فإن التشخص الذهني الذي عليه مدار الجزئية إنما هو بنحو الإدراك، وهو الإحساس لا التعقل ، ولا يخفى أن الأشبه ما قد أسلفنا سابقا.
، وهو الحق بحسب دقيق النظر ، وإن كان جلي النظر يحكم بالأول؛
Universality and particularity? Or an attribute? Commentary of Bahr al-Ulum on Sullam al-Ulum: Demonstration. This is what the majority of investigators have accepted, like al-Sayyid al-Muhaqqiq (may his noble secret be sanctified) and al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawwani. And the most apparent is that they are of two types: one that has possible non-existent individuals, and it is true of them after the assumption of existence, like the phoenix and all possible non-existent concepts, and their universality is with respect to them; and one that has no individual at all, like the concept of necessary of both sides and all impossibilities, and their universality is in relation to realities both existent and non-existent together. (This) And God knows best what is correct. And universality and particularity are attributes of the known, as we explained earlier, and it was said they are attributes of knowledge. Attribute of the known, and the verification of this statement: that particularity follows a mode of existence according to the proponents of this statement. Knowledge, then the particularity that occurs to a concept in the mind only distinguishes it from other mental forms, not from external individuals, unlike external determination; for it follows external existence, so it distinguishes it from other external individuals. Thus the mental form particularized by mental particularity does not prevent the mind, by virtue of its very conception, from assuming it shared among external individuals, meaning that if it existed it would be the very external individuals of its kind, unlike sensory and imaginal forms; because they contain an identity preventing multiplicity in the external. If you say: just as sharing in mental forms is permissible, why is sharing of the external particular in forms occurring in sense not permissible, so that it could occur without abstraction in the mind? I say: if the permissibility of sharing according to the conception itself is granted, it does not entail the possibility of occurrence in actual fact; due to the occurrence of external accidents that are contrary to mental particularity. This is the fullest exposition of their speech, and to this refers what is in the gloss: that is the doctrine of the ancients, for mental particularity upon which particularity revolves is only by way of perception, and it is sensation, not intellection. And it is not hidden that the more similar is what we have previously mentioned. And it is the truth according to precise investigation, although superficial investigation judges the first.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage begins by referencing the majority view on demonstration, then states that universals are of two types: those with possible existent individuals (like the phoenix) and those with no individuals (like impossible concepts). It then discusses whether universality/particularity are attributes of the known object or of knowledge. The author settles on attribute of the known, explaining that mental particularity only distinguishes concepts in the mind, not in external existence. An objection is raised about sharing in sensory forms, and the answer is that external accidents prevent sharing in actual existence. The passage concludes that the correct view according to precise investigation is that universality/particularity are attributes of the known, though common view holds otherwise.

Definition of the Particular: Real and Relative
Defines the particular as known through sensation and distinguishes real from relative particular.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the real particular (al-juz'ī al-ḥaqīqī) from the relative particular (al-juz'ī al-iḍāfī), and explain why the real particular is known through sensation.

Core Idea
The particular is either a sensory individual or a concept relative to a broader universal.

The study of the particular (al-juz'ī) is essential for understanding how universals and particulars are categorized in logic. In this passage, the author defines the particular and clarifies two distinct types: the real particular, which is directly perceived by the senses, and the relative particular, which is any concept that falls under a more general universal. This distinction helps avoid confusion when discussing terms like 'Zayd' (a real particular) versus 'human' (a relative particular when compared to 'animal').

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions given in the passage.
The passage first defines the particular as something that is neither kāsib nor muktasab—i.e., not acquired by reasoning. Then it adds a second usage: al-juz'ī also refers to anything subsumed under a more general universal. These two definitions correspond to the real particular and the relative particular.
2
Understand 'kāsib' and 'muktasab'.
The terms kāsib (acquirer) and muktasab (acquired) emphasize that the real particular is not obtained through intellectual effort; immediate sensation suffices. For example, seeing a specific person requires no deduction.
This shows the epistemological basis for distinguishing particulars.
3
Distinguish real versus relative particular.
The real particular (e.g., 'this Zayd') is a concrete individual known by sense. The relative particular (e.g., 'human' relative to 'animal') is a concept that is more specific than a broader universal but still universal in itself. The passage says the relative particular is called juz'ī only by relation.
The relative particular is also a universal in other contexts—this is key for the five universals.
4
Apply the distinction to an Islamic example.
Consider the term 'this specific prayer' (real particular) versus 'prayer' in general (relative particular when compared to 'acts of worship'). The real particular is known by performing it, while 'prayer' as a concept is a relative particular under 'worship'.
This illustrates how the distinction functions in fiqh discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( وَالْجُزْئِيُّ لَا يَكُونُ كَاسِبًا وَلَا مُكْتَسَباً ؛ لأن الإحساس كاف في إدراكه. (وَقَدْ يُقَالُ الْجُزْنِيُّ ](١) لِكُلِّ مُنْدَرج تَحْتَ كُلِّيِّ أَعَمَّ(٢)، وَيَخْتَصُّ بِالْإِضَافِي كَالْأَوَّلِ بِالْحَقِيقِي (٣)) .
And the particular is neither a kāsib nor a muktasab; because sensation is sufficient for its perception. And it is said al-juz'ī for everything subsumed under a more general universal, and it is distinguished by the relative just as the first is distinguished by the real.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage is a definitional statement from the matn, commented upon by the author. It defines the particular (al-juz'ī) in two ways. First, the real particular (al-ḥaqīqī) is that which cannot be acquired by reasoning (kāsib) or be the result of acquisition (muktasab) because it is directly known through sensation. That is, particulars like 'this Zayd' are perceived by the senses, not by logical deduction. Second, a relative particular (al-iḍāfī) is any concept that falls under a more general universal, such as 'human' relative to 'animal'. The term 'al-juz'ī' is applied to both, but the first is the true particular, while the second is particular only by relation. This distinction is important for understanding how universals and particulars are categorized in logic.

Key Takeaway
The particular is either a sensory individual (real particular) or a concept subsumed under a broader universal (relative particular), and the real particular is known directly by sensation, not by reasoning.

Identifying Total Coextensiveness Between Universals
Defining total coextensiveness between universals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when two universals are totally coextensive and produce the corresponding universal affirmative propositions.

Core Idea
Total coextensiveness means two universals cover exactly the same individuals.

Logicians focus on universals because logic deals with universal concepts, not individual instances. The first relation between two universals is total coextensiveness (al-tasāduq al-kullī). This lesson will show you how to recognize when two universals apply to the same set of individuals, using the condition that both directions of implication hold.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two universals
Look at the two terms you want to compare. For example, 'human' (insān) and 'rational animal' (hayawān nāṭiq).
2
Check the first direction
Ask: Is every instance of the first universal also an instance of the second? For 'human' and 'rational animal', every human is a rational animal.
This gives a universal affirmative proposition: 'Every human is a rational animal.'
3
Check the reverse direction
Ask: Is every instance of the second universal also an instance of the first? For 'rational animal', every rational animal is human.
This gives the reverse universal affirmative: 'Every rational animal is a human.'
4
Confirm total coextensiveness
If both directions hold, the two universals are totally coextensive. If only one direction holds, it is a different relation (general-special).
Total coextensiveness requires both sides, as the footnote (4) indicates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مبحث : النِّسَبُ بَيْنَ الكَليَّاتِ] ولما لم يكن للمنطقي شغل بالجزئيات خص البحث بالكليات، وشرع فيه فقال: (الْكُلِّيَّانِ إِنْ تَصَادَقَا كُليًّا (٤) ، بأن ينعقد منهما موجبتان كليتان.
Section on the relations between universals; and since the logician has no concern with particulars, he devotes his investigation to universals. He began by saying: 'The two universals, if they are coextensive totally, meaning that two universal affirmative propositions are formed from them.'
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of a new section in the matn and commentary dealing with the relations between universals (al-nisab bayn al-kulliyyāt). It first justifies why logicians focus on universals rather than particulars: because logic deals with universal concepts about things, not individual instances. The matn then defines the first relation: total coextensiveness (al-tasāduq kulliyyan). Two universals are said to be totally coextensive when they apply to exactly the same set of individuals. For example, 'human' and 'rational animal' are coextensive; every human is a rational animal and every rational animal is a human. The commentator explains that this means that from them two universal affirmative propositions can be formed, such as 'Every human is a rational animal' and 'Every rational animal is a human'. The footnote (4) indicates that this coextensiveness must be from both sides, not only one, to distinguish it from other relations like general-special.

Key Takeaway
Total coextensiveness is a relation between two universals that requires both directions of inclusion, allowing conversion of universal affirmative propositions.

Definition of General and Specific Negations of Possible
Author defines negations of possible and resolves a logical problem.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'la mumkin 'amm' and 'la mumkin khass' and explain the logical condition that resolves the doubt about their relationship.

Core Idea
The negation of a contradictory term does not generate a true proposition unless the term has instances.

In this passage, the author defines two important modal terms: 'general not-possible' and 'specific not-possible'. He then shows how a doubt about their relationship is resolved by a key logical condition: the attributive description must apply to something for a proposition to be meaningful. This lesson will guide you through the definitions and the resolution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
Locate the Arabic terms 'لا يمكن عام' and 'لا يمكن خاص' in the text. Understand that 'لا يمكن عام' negates general possibility and is equivalent to necessary or impossible. 'لا يمكن خاص' is the negation of specific possibility, also equivalent to necessary or impossible.
2
Understand the relationship
The author states that every 'لا يمكن عام' is a 'لا يمكن خاص'. This is derived by the converse ('بالعكس') of a previous relationship. Recognize that this implies a hierarchical relation.
3
Recognize the doubt
If the relationship fails, then some 'لا يمكن عام' would be 'ممكن خاص' (specific possible), which contradicts the definition of 'لا يمكن عام'. This is a potential counterexample.
4
Apply the resolution condition
The author resolves the doubt by noting that the attributive description ('الوصف العنواني') must be true of a subject for a proposition to be formed. Since 'لا يمكن عام' is a contradictory term that has no individuals, the conditional 'if it is not true that every ... then some ...' cannot be evaluated because the antecedent is false in a vacuous sense.
The condition prevents logical paradoxes arising from empty terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في المعاني المفردة
بالعكس، فَكُلُّ لا مُمْكِن عَامَ لامُمُكن خاص ، وكُلُّ لايمكن خاص إنا وَاجِبٌ أَوْ مُمْتَنِعٌ ؛ لأن كل لاممكن خاص إما ضروري الوجود أو ضروري العدم، فكل لاممكن عام إما واجب أو ممتنع ، وكلاهما ممكن عام . انحل لَا مُمْكِن عَامَ مُمْكِنْ عَامٌ ، والجواب بما مر، من تخصيص بغير نقائض المفهومات الشاملة .
لا يقال : لو لم يصدق كل لاممكن عام لاممكن خاص يصدق بعض لاممكن عام ممكن خاص هذا خلف، فالشك بحاله ؛ لأن اللاممكن العام لا يصدق على شيء؛ حتى يصدق عليه الممكن الخاص أو نقيضه ؛ إذ صدق الوصف العنواني شرط في انعقاد القضية، وبهذا ينحل الشبهة بأن ضروري الطرفين ليس بموجود، فبعض ما ليس بموجود ضروري الطرفين. ثم نقول : كل ما ليس بموجود إما ممكن أو ممتنع ، وكلاهما ليس بضروري الطرفين. فلا شيء مما ليس بموجود ضروري الطرفين، وهو متناقض للأول، وكذا انحل الشبهة بأن الذي وجوده وعدمه يستلزم المحال إما موجود أو معدوم، وعلى كلا التقديرين يلزم المحال .
In singular meanings. By the converse, then every not-possible general is not-possible specific, and every not-possible specific is either necessary or impossible; because every not-possible specific is either necessary of existence or necessary of non-existence. So every not-possible general is either necessary or impossible, and both are possible general. The problem is resolved: 'not-possible general is possible general' and the answer is as passed, by specification except for the opposites of comprehensive concepts. It is not said: If it is not true that every not-possible general is not-possible specific, it would be true that some not-possible general is possible specific, which is a contradiction; so the doubt remains. Because the not-possible general is not true of anything such that the specific possible or its opposite is true of it; because the truth of the attributive description is a condition for the forming of the proposition. And by this the doubt is resolved: that the necessary of two extremes does not exist, so some of what does not exist is necessary of two extremes. Then we say: everything that does not exist is either possible or impossible, and neither is necessary of two extremes, so nothing that does not exist is necessary of two extremes, which contradicts the first. Similarly, the doubt is resolved: that that whose existence and non-existence entail impossibility is either existent or non-existent, and on both suppositions impossibility follows.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the Sullam al-Ulum. The author defines the terms 'لا يمكن عام' (general not-possible) and 'لا يمكن خاص' (specific not-possible). He states that every general not-possible is a specific not-possible, and every specific not-possible is either necessary or impossible. This leads to the conclusion that general not-possible is either necessary or impossible, both of which are general possible. Then a doubt is raised: if it is not true that every general not-possible is specific not-possible, then some general not-possible would be specific possible, which is contradictory. The author resolves this by noting that the attributive description (الوصف العنواني) must be true of something for a proposition to hold. Since 'general not-possible' does not apply to anything (because it is a contradictory concept), the conditional proposition fails. He then resolves a related doubt about necessary of two extremes (ضروري الطرفين) not being existent, and the conclusion that nothing non-existent is necessary of two extremes, which contradicts an initial claim. This shows the importance of the condition of existence for logical relations.

Key Takeaway
The logical condition that the subject of a proposition must exist prevents contradictions when dealing with negations of contradictory terms.

Negations of General and Specific from One Aspect
Author defines negation relationships with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the logical relationship between the negations of two concepts based on their original relationship (partial divergence, total divergence, or generality from one aspect).

Core Idea
Negations invert relationships: partial overlap yields partial divergence, total divergence yields partial overlap.

When two concepts are related as general and specific from one aspect (partial overlap), their negations are not simply the reverse. Instead, they become partially divergent. The commentator explains this principle with concrete examples, showing how the relationship between negations depends on the original relationship between the concepts. Understanding this helps in analyzing definitions and logical arguments in all Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Understand the base claim
The matn claims: 'Between the two negations of the more general and the more specific from one aspect there is partial divergence.' Grasp that the negations of two concepts with partial overlap are themselves partially divergent, not fully overlapping or fully separate.
2
Analyze the first example: not-stone and not-animal
Consider 'not-stone' and 'not-animal'. Their originals (stone and animal) are totally divergent. But the negations have generality from one aspect: 'not-stone' includes animals and other things; 'not-animal' includes stones and other things. They overlap partially.
This shows partial divergence within total divergence.
3
Analyze the second example: human and not-rational
Here, 'human' and 'not-rational' are totally divergent (nothing is both human and not-rational). Their negations 'not-human' and 'rational' are also totally divergent. This is a case of total divergence in both original and negations.
Note: the author gives this as a contrasting case.
4
Analyze the third example: white and human
These two concepts are general and specific from one aspect: some whites are humans, some are not; some humans are white, some are not. Their negations 'not-white' and 'not-human' also have the same relationship (generality from one aspect).
This shows partial divergence within partial overlap.
5
Summarize the pattern
From the examples, deduce: The relationship between negations is not the same as the relationship between originals. When originals have partial overlap, their negations are partially divergent. When originals have total divergence, their negations may be partially overlapping or totally divergent depending on the case.

نقيضي الأعم (وَبَيْنَ نَقِيضَيْ الْأَعَمِّ وَالْأَخَصَّ مِنْ وَجْه تبايِّنُ جُزْتَيَّ كَالْمُتباين). أي النسبة بين الأعم والأخص بين نقيضي المتباينين تباين جزئي، كذلك بين نقيضي من وجه، (وَهُوَ التَّفَارُقُ)، أي تفارق كل واحد من الكليين عن الآخر في الجملة إما كليا أو جزئيا ; لأن بين العينين تفارقا، فحيث يصدق عين أحدهما، يصدق نقيض الآخر، فيصدق كل من النقيضين مع عين الآخر ، فلا يصدق مع نقيضه، (وَهُوَ)، أي التباين الجزئي (قَدْ يَتَحَقَّقُ في ضِمْنِ التَبايُنِ الْكُلِّيِّ، كَاللَّا حَجَرِ وَاللَّا حَيَوَانِ) ، فبينهما عموم من وجه وبين نقيضيهما - وهما الحجر والإنسان ـ تباين كلي، (وَالْإِنْسَانِ وَاللَّانَاطِقِ)، بينهما تباين كلي، وكذا بين نقيضيهما، وهما اللا إنسان والناطق. (وَقَدْ يَتَحَقَّقُ فِي ضِمْنِ الْعُمُومِ مِنْ وَجْهِ، كَالْأَبْيَضِ وَالْإِنْسَانِ)، بينهما عموم من وجه، وكذا بين نقيضيهما: اللاأبيض واللا إنسان، (وَالْحَجَرِ وَالْحَيَوَانِ)، بينهما تباين كلي، وبين نقيضيهما - هما اللاحجر واللاحيوان ـ عموم من وجه.
The two negations of the more general and the more specific from one aspect: (And between the two negations of the more general and the more specific from one aspect there is partial divergence like between two mutually exclusive (concepts)). Meaning: the relationship between the more general and the more specific is like the relationship between the negations of two mutually exclusive concepts: partial divergence. Likewise between the negations of (concepts related) from one aspect. And it (i.e., partial divergence) is the separation, meaning the separation of each of the two universals from the other, either wholly or partially. Because between the two essences there is separation, so wherever one essence is true, the negation of the other is true. So each of the two negations is true with the other essence, but not with its negation. And it (i.e., partial divergence) may occur within the framework of total divergence, like 'not-stone' and 'not-animal'. Between them there is generality from one aspect, and between their two negations—namely stone and human—there is total divergence. And (like) 'human' and 'not-rational'—between them there is total divergence, and likewise between their two negations, namely 'not-human' and 'rational'. And it may occur within the framework of generality from one aspect, like 'white' and 'human'—between them there is generality from one aspect, and likewise between their two negations: 'not-white' and 'not-human'. And (like) 'stone' and 'animal'—between them there is total divergence, and between their two negations—namely 'not-stone' and 'not-animal'—there is generality from one aspect.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage is a commentary that expands on a statement from the matn about the relationship between the negations (naqidayn) of two concepts that are general and specific from one aspect (a'amm wa akhass min wajh). The commentator explains that the relationship between these negations is partial divergence (tabayun juz'i), meaning they overlap partially but not fully. He illustrates that this is like the relationship between the negations of two mutually exclusive (mutabayin) concepts, which are also partially divergent. The key is that wherever one of the original essences (ayn) is true, the negation of the other is true, but not the other way around. Then he gives examples: For 'not-stone' and 'not-animal', they have generality from one aspect, but their negations (stone and human) are totally divergent. For 'human' and 'not-rational', they are totally divergent, and their negations are also totally divergent. For 'white' and 'human', they have generality from one aspect, and their negations also have generality from one aspect. For 'stone' and 'animal', they are totally divergent, but their negations have generality from one aspect. This shows how the relationship between negations depends on the relationship between the original concepts.

A) The negations are also totally divergent.

C) The negations are identical.
D) There is no definite relationship.

Key Takeaway
The relationship between negations is not a mirror of the original relationship; partial divergence often appears where total divergence existed.

The Three Modes of Consideration for Quiddities
Author explains three modes of considering quiddities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three modes of considering a quiddity and explain the logical relation (identity, difference, or predication) associated with each.

Core Idea
How you consider a quiddity determines whether it is the same, different, or predicable.

In this passage, the author introduces three ways to view the relationship between a determined quiddity (al-muhassal) and its determiner (al-muhassil). These modes are essential for understanding how genus, differentia, species, and accidents relate in logic and Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Three Arabic Terms
Find the three Arabic phrases: بشرط شيء (with condition of a thing), بشرط لا (with condition of nothing), and لا بشرط (without any condition). They correspond to different logical treatments of quiddities.
2
Understand the Condition for Each Mode
For bi-sharti shay', the quiddity is taken with the condition of being determined by a specific determiner. For bi-sharti lā, it is taken with the condition of not being determined. For lā bi-shart, no condition is attached.
The term 'muhassal' refers to the quiddity being determined, and 'muhassil' is the determiner.
3
Determine the Logical Outcome
When taken bi-sharti shay', the quiddity is identical in existence to the determiner. When bi-sharti lā, it is distinct and not predicated. When lā bi-shart, it is predicated, fluctuating between identity and difference.
The text says: 'عينه في الوجود' (identical), 'مغايرة له في الوجود غير محمولة' (different, not predicated), and 'محمولة عليه' (predicated).
4
Apply to an Example from Islamic Thought
Consider the quiddity 'knowledge' and 'power' in the context of Allah's attributes. Taken with condition of being determined by the Essence, they are identical to the Essence; taken with condition of not being determined, they are distinct; taken without condition, they are predicated of the Essence.
This example is from Kalam discussions on divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الكليات الخمس
المحصل، متزلزلة بين أن يكون هذا أو غيره، فهي إذا أخذت بشرط شيء، أي بشرط التحصل بهذا المحصل، فهي عينه في الوجود، فإذا أخذت بشرط لا شي. شيء، أي بشرط عدم التحصل، بل على أنها ماهية مستقلة مغايرة له، فهي بهذا الاعتبار مغايرة له في الوجود، غير محمولة عليه، وإذا أخذت لا بشرط شيء، أي التحصل وعدمه فهي محمولة عليه ؛ لوجود الاعتبارين.
ثم منها ما يقع في الوجود الواقعي، ليكمل في ذاتها بالتحصل، ويتحصل منها ومن المحصل ذات يطلب كمالاتها ، فيقال لها ولمحصلها جنس وفصل، إذا أخذتا لا بشرط شيء، ومادة وصورة إذا أخذتا بشرط لا ، ونوع إذا أخذ أحدهما بشرط الآخر.
ومنها: ما يقع في الوجود على أنها كمال للغير، وهي عرض إذا أخذت بشرط لا، وقد تكون موجودة في الخارج، كالسواد، وقد لا تكون، لكن للعقل أن يأخذها بشرط ،لا، وهي المبادئ الانتزاعية، وعرض إذا أخذت لا بشرط شيء، ومحمول عليه، واستبعادات اتحاد الاثنين مطلقا غير لائق، بل إذا كانا متحصلين .
ثم اعلم أن هذه الاعتبارات الثلث كما يجري في الجنس بالنسبة إلى الفصل، كذلك يجري في الفصل بالنسبة إلى الجنس، وفيهما بالنسبة إلى النوع ، وفي النوع بالنسبة إلى الشخص، فالنوع إذا أخذ بشرط التشخص كان شخصا أو حصة أو فردا، وإن أخذ بشرط عدمه كان مباينا لهذا بهذا الاعتبار ، لكنها بهذا الاعتبار لا وجود لها إلا في اللحاظ، وإن أخذ بشرط شيء كان محمولا عليه، وكما أنها تجري في الأعراض بالنسبة إلى المحال، كذلك تجري في المحال بالنسبة إلى الأعراض، فالمحال إذا أخذت بشرط شيء وهو العرض - كان متحدا معه، وإن أخذ بشرط لا كان مغايرا له. ولهذه المرتبة وجود إذا كان الأعراض أعراضا مفارقة، وأما إذا كانت لازمة فلا وجود لها إلا في اعتبار العقل.
وإذا أخذ لا بشرط شيء كان متحدا معه ومحمولا عليه. ثم الاتحاد لا يمكن إلا بين مبهم ومتحصل أو مبهمين ؛ إذ الاتحاد من الجانبين.
وما أورد من المعارضات والاستبعادات فينكشف حالها في التركيب الاتحادي، إن شاء الله تعالى. فقد ظهر أن الماهية المتحدة مع أخرى إذا أخذت بشرط شيء ء كانت عين الأخرى، وإذا أخذت بشرط لا كانت غيرها، وإذا أخذت لا بشرط شيء كانت متزلزلة بين أن يكون عينها أو غيرها، فهي ذات جهتين مغايرة لها ومتحدة معها ؛ فإنها إذا اعتبرت بشرط شيء فهي متحدة، وإذا أخذت بشرط لا فهي مباينة في الوجود، فقد ظهر أن حقيقة الحمل المتعارف اتحاد المتغايرين بنحو ، يعني إذا أخذ أحدهما بشرط لا في نحو آخر، يعني إذا أخذ أحدهما بشرط الآخر فالمحمول بالذات ما يكون مغايرا لموضوعه في نحو من الوجود ومتحدا معه في نحو آخر منه، وبان أيضا أن الجزئي لا يحمل على آخر .
A chapter on the five universals. The determined (muhassal) is fluctuating between being this or something else. So when it is taken with a condition (bi-sharti shay'), i.e., with the condition of determination by this determiner (muhassil), it is identical to it in existence. When it is taken with the condition of nothing (bi-sharti la shay'), i.e., with the condition of lack of determination, but as an independent quiddity different from it, then in this respect it is different from it in existence, not predicated upon it. And when it is taken without any condition (la bi-sharti shay'), i.e., determination and its absence, then it is predicated upon it because of the existence of two considerations. Then among them is what occurs in real existence, to be completed in its essence through determination, and from it and the determiner arises an essence that seeks its perfections, so it and its determiner are called genus and differentia when taken without any condition, matter and form when taken with the condition of nothing, and species when one of them is taken with the condition of the other. And among them is what occurs in existence as a perfection for another, which is an accident ('arad) when taken with the condition of nothing, and sometimes it exists externally like blackness, sometimes not, but the intellect can take it with the condition of nothing—these are the abstractive principles—and it is an accidental (aradi) when taken without any condition, and predicated upon it. Absolutely denying the union of two is not appropriate; rather [it is denied] when they are both determined. Then know that these three considerations apply to the genus in relation to the differentia, and likewise to the differentia in relation to the genus, and to both in relation to the species, and to the species in relation to the individual. For the species, if taken with the condition of individuation, it is an individual, a portion, or a particular; if taken with the condition of absence of individuation, it is disparate from this in this respect, but in this respect it has no existence except in mental consideration; and if taken with a condition, it is predicated upon it. And just as they apply to accidents in relation to subjects, likewise they apply to subjects in relation to accidents. For the subject, if taken with the condition of the accident, it is identical with it; if taken with the condition of nothing, it is different from it. For this rank, there is existence when the accidents are separable, but if they are inseparable, they have no existence except in mental consideration. And if taken without any condition, it is identical with it and predicated upon it. Then union is only possible between an indeterminate and a determined, or between two indeterminates, because union is from both sides. Whatever objections and improbabilities are raised, their truth will be revealed in the unitive composition, God willing. So it has become clear that the quiddity united with another, if taken with a condition, is identical to the other; if taken with the condition of nothing, it is other than it; and if taken without any condition, it fluctuates between being identical to it or other than it. Thus it has two aspects: being different from it and being united with it. For if it is considered with a condition, it is united; if taken with the condition of nothing, it is separate in existence. So it has become clear that the reality of conventional predication is the union of two different things in a certain manner, meaning when one of them is taken with the condition of nothing in another manner, i.e., when one of them is taken with the condition of the other. So the essential predicate is what is different from its subject in one mode of existence and united with it in another mode. And it also became clear that the particular is not predicated upon another particular.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory explanation of the three modes of consideration (اعتبارات) that apply to quiddities (ماهيات) and their relations. The author begins by introducing a quiddity that is 'determined' (محصل) by another quiddity (المحصل). He outlines three ways to consider this determined quiddity: first, taken with the condition of being determined by the determiner (بشرط شيء) — in which case it is identical to the determiner in existence; second, taken with the condition of not being determined (بشرط لا شيء) — in which case it is an independent quiddity different from the determiner and not predicated upon it; third, taken without any condition (لا بشرط شيء) — in which case it is predicated upon the determiner, fluctuating between identity and difference. The author then applies these three considerations to the logical relations: genus and differentia, when taken absolutely (لا بشرط), are predicable of the species; when taken with the condition of not being the other (بشرط لا), they are matter and form; when one is taken with the condition of the other (بشرط شيء), they constitute the species. Similarly, for accidents: an accident taken with the condition of not being anything (بشرط لا) is a category of accident (e.g., quality); taken absolutely (لا بشرط), it is an accidental property (عرضي) predicated on the subject. The author then extends the three considerations to the relation between species and individual, and between subject and accident. For example, a species taken with the condition of individuation becomes a particular individual; taken with the condition of not being individuated, it is disparate; taken absolutely, it is predicated on the individual. For the subject (محل) and accident, the subject taken with the condition of the accident is identical with it, taken with the condition of not being the accident is different, and taken absolutely is predicated. The author concludes that genuine predication involves a union of two different things under different modes of consideration: the predicate is different from the subject in one aspect (when taken with the condition of not being the subject) but united in another (when taken with the condition of being predicated). An Islamic example: the quiddity 'animal' and 'rational' — considered absolutely, 'rational' is predicated of man; considered with the condition of not being animal, it is the differentia separate from genus. This clarifies that the same quiddity can have multiple relationships based on how it is considered.

Key Terms
بشرط شيء
بشرط لا
لا بشرط
محصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when a quiddity is taken 'بشرط شيء' (with condition of determination), what is its relation to the determined thing?
A) It is different in existence
B) It is identical in existence
C) It is neither identical nor different
D) It is predicated upon it

Key Takeaway
A quiddity taken with condition of determination is identical; taken with condition of no determination is different; taken without condition is predicated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task: Take the quiddity 'knowledge' and the quiddity 'essence' in the context of Allah's attributes. For each of the three modes (bi-sharti shay', bi-sharti lā, lā bi-shart), state the logical relation (identical, different, or predicated) and provide a brief justification based on the passage.
?
Task: Identify which mode is being used when a mutakallim says 'Allah's knowledge is His essence' and when another says 'Allah's knowledge is distinct from His essence'. Explain how the same reality can accommodate both statements.

Applying the Three Modes: Genus, Differentia, and Species
Applying three modes to genus, differentia, and species

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how genus and differentia are understood under each of the three modes and identify their corresponding terms (genus-differentia, matter-form, species).

Core Idea
Genus and differentia are not fixed; their relationship depends on the mode of consideration.

The three modes of consideration are not limited to simple quiddities; they also govern the relationships between fundamental logical concepts like genus, differentia, and species. The passage shows how the same pair of quiddities can be seen as genus and differentia, matter and form, or species depending on the mode.

Guided Path
1
Recall the Three Modes
Remember: bi-sharti shay' (with condition of determination) → identity; bi-sharti lā (with condition of nothing) → difference; lā bi-shart (without condition) → predication.
2
Apply to Genus and Differentia
When the determined quiddity and its determiner (e.g., 'animal' and 'rational') are taken without any condition (lā bi-shart), they are called genus and differentia respectively and are predicated of the species. When taken with the condition of nothing (bi-sharti lā), they are called matter and form (mādda wa ṣūra) and are distinct. When one is taken with the condition of the other (bi-sharti shay'), they unite to form the species.
The text says: 'فيقال لها ولمحصلها جنس وفصل، إذا أخذتا لا بشرط شيء، ومادة وصورة إذا أخذتا بشرط لا، ونوع إذا أخذ أحدهما بشرط الآخر.'
3
Extend to Species and Individual
Similarly, the species in relation to the individual: taken with condition of individuation → individual; taken with condition of no individuation → disparate (mental only); taken without condition → predicated.
The passage also applies the modes to subject-accident relations.
4
Summarise the Logical Flexibility
The same quiddities can be thought of as genus, differentia, matter, form, or species depending on the mode. This shows that logical relations are not absolute but depend on how we consider the terms.

فصل في الكليات الخمس
المحصل، متزلزلة بين أن يكون هذا أو غيره، فهي إذا أخذت بشرط شيء، أي بشرط التحصل بهذا المحصل، فهي عينه في الوجود، فإذا أخذت بشرط لا شي. شيء، أي بشرط عدم التحصل، بل على أنها ماهية مستقلة مغايرة له، فهي بهذا الاعتبار مغايرة له في الوجود، غير محمولة عليه، وإذا أخذت لا بشرط شيء، أي التحصل وعدمه فهي محمولة عليه ؛ لوجود الاعتبارين.

وإذا أخذ لا بشرط شيء كان متحدا معه ومحمولا عليه. ثم الاتحاد لا يمكن إلا بين مبهم ومتحصل أو مبهمين ؛ إذ الاتحاد من الجانبين.
وما أورد من المعارضات والاستبعادات فينكشف حالها في التركيب الاتحادي، إن شاء الله تعالى. فقد ظهر أن الماهية المتحدة مع أخرى إذا أخذت بشرط شيء ء كانت عين الأخرى، وإذا أخذت بشرط لا كانت غيرها، وإذا أخذت لا بشرط شيء كانت متزلزلة بين أن يكون عينها أو غيرها، فهي ذات جهتين مغايرة لها ومتحدة معها ؛ فإنها إذا اعتبرت بشرط شيء فهي متحدة، وإذا أخذت بشرط لا فهي مباينة في الوجود، فقد ظهر أن حقيقة الحمل المتعارف اتحاد المتغايرين بنحو ، يعني إذا أخذ أحدهما بشرط لا في نحو آخر، يعني إذا أخذ أحدهما بشرط الآخر فالمحمول بالذات ما يكون مغايرا لموضوعه في نحو من الوجود ومتحدا معه في نحو آخر منه، وبان أيضا أن الجزئي لا يحمل على آخر .
A chapter on the five universals. The determined (muhassal) is fluctuating between being this or something else. So when it is taken with a condition (bi-sharti shay'), i.e., with the condition of determination by this determiner (muhassil), it is identical to it in existence. When it is taken with the condition of nothing (bi-sharti la shay'), i.e., with the condition of lack of determination, but as an independent quiddity different from it, then in this respect it is different from it in existence, not predicated upon it. And when it is taken without any condition (la bi-sharti shay'), i.e., determination and its absence, then it is predicated upon it because of the existence of two considerations. Then among them is what occurs in real existence, to be completed in its essence through determination, and from it and the determiner arises an essence that seeks its perfections, so it and its determiner are called genus and differentia when taken without any condition, matter and form when taken with the condition of nothing, and species when one of them is taken with the condition of the other. And among them is what occurs in existence as a perfection for another, which is an accident ('arad) when taken with the condition of nothing, and sometimes it exists externally like blackness, sometimes not, but the intellect can take it with the condition of nothing—these are the abstractive principles—and it is an accidental (aradi) when taken without any condition, and predicated upon it. Absolutely denying the union of two is not appropriate; rather [it is denied] when they are both determined. Then know that these three considerations apply to the genus in relation to the differentia, and likewise to the differentia in relation to the genus, and to both in relation to the species, and to the species in relation to the individual. For the species, if taken with the condition of individuation, it is an individual, a portion, or a particular; if taken with the condition of absence of individuation, it is disparate from this in this respect, but in this respect it has no existence except in mental consideration; and if taken with a condition, it is predicated upon it. And just as they apply to accidents in relation to subjects, likewise they apply to subjects in relation to accidents. For the subject, if taken with the condition of the accident, it is identical with it; if taken with the condition of nothing, it is different from it. For this rank, there is existence when the accidents are separable, but if they are inseparable, they have no existence except in mental consideration. And if taken without any condition, it is identical with it and predicated upon it. Then union is only possible between an indeterminate and a determined, or between two indeterminates, because union is from both sides. Whatever objections and improbabilities are raised, their truth will be revealed in the unitive composition, God willing. So it has become clear that the quiddity united with another, if taken with a condition, is identical to the other; if taken with the condition of nothing, it is other than it; and if taken without any condition, it fluctuates between being identical to it or other than it. Thus it has two aspects: being different from it and being united with it. For if it is considered with a condition, it is united; if taken with the condition of nothing, it is separate in existence. So it has become clear that the reality of conventional predication is the union of two different things in a certain manner, meaning when one of them is taken with the condition of nothing in another manner, i.e., when one of them is taken with the condition of the other. So the essential predicate is what is different from its subject in one mode of existence and united with it in another mode. And it also became clear that the particular is not predicated upon another particular.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory explanation of the three modes of consideration (اعتبارات) that apply to quiddities (ماهيات) and their relations. The author begins by introducing a quiddity that is 'determined' (محصل) by another quiddity (المحصل). He outlines three ways to consider this determined quiddity: first, taken with the condition of being determined by the determiner (بشرط شيء) — in which case it is identical to the determiner in existence; second, taken with the condition of not being determined (بشرط لا شيء) — in which case it is an independent quiddity different from the determiner and not predicated upon it; third, taken without any condition (لا بشرط شيء) — in which case it is predicated upon the determiner, fluctuating between identity and difference. The author then applies these three considerations to the logical relations: genus and differentia, when taken absolutely (لا بشرط), are predicable of the species; when taken with the condition of not being the other (بشرط لا), they are matter and form; when one is taken with the condition of the other (بشرط شيء), they constitute the species. Similarly, for accidents: an accident taken with the condition of not being anything (بشرط لا) is a category of accident (e.g., quality); taken absolutely (لا بشرط), it is an accidental property (عرضي) predicated on the subject. The author then extends the three considerations to the relation between species and individual, and between subject and accident. For example, a species taken with the condition of individuation becomes a particular individual; taken with the condition of not being individuated, it is disparate; taken absolutely, it is predicated on the individual. For the subject (محل) and accident, the subject taken with the condition of the accident is identical with it, taken with the condition of not being the accident is different, and taken absolutely is predicated. The author concludes that genuine predication involves a union of two different things under different modes of consideration: the predicate is different from the subject in one aspect (when taken with the condition of not being the subject) but united in another (when taken with the condition of being predicated). An Islamic example: the quiddity 'animal' and 'rational' — considered absolutely, 'rational' is predicated of man; considered with the condition of not being animal, it is the differentia separate from genus. This clarifies that the same quiddity can have multiple relationships based on how it is considered.

Key Terms
جنس
فصل
مادة
صورة
نوع
محصل

Key Takeaway
The same logical components (genus and differentia) are called genus-differentia, matter-form, or species based on which of the three modes is applied.

Al-Dawani's Modal Distinction: Accident vs. Accidental Property
Al-Dawani clarifies the distinction between accident and accidental using the example of 'white'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between accident (عرض) and accidental property (عرضي) using al-Dawani's three-case analysis of 'white' and apply this modal distinction to any predicate in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Accident and accidental are the same nature seen under different conditions: absolute vs. restricted.

In this passage, al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawani provides a precise clarification of the difference between accident as a category (عرض) and accidental property as a predicate (عرضي). Using the concrete example of 'white', he shows that the same quiddity can be considered either as an accident inhering in a subject or as a property attributed to a subject, depending on whether we take it with or without conditions. This modal distinction is essential for understanding the five universals and how definitions work in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term al-Dawani is analyzing
Recognize that al-Dawani uses the word 'white' (الأبيض) as a test case. This is a concrete example of an accident.
2
Extract the three modes of consideration
Note the three conditional phrases: (a) taken without any condition (لا بشرط شيء) -> accidental property, (b) taken with the condition of something (بشرط شيء) -> the white garment (a concrete object), (c) taken with the condition of nothing (بشرط لا شيء) -> the accident opposite to substance.
The first and third are the key pair for the distinction.
3
Compare the first and third modes
The first mode (without condition) yields a predicate (عرضي) that can be said of a subject like 'the body is white'. The third mode (with condition of nothing) yields the accident itself (عرض) considered as a category, e.g., 'whiteness' as a quality.
4
Understand the analogy to the essential (ذاتي)
Al-Dawani draws a parallel: just as the essential nature (like animal) can be considered as genus and matter (relative to differentia) or as differentia and form (relative to genus), the accident nature can be considered as accident (category) or accidental property (predicate) in two respects. This shows the importance of modality.
5
Apply the distinction to a new example
Take 'knowledge'. Without condition: 'Zayd is knowledgeable' (عرضي). With condition of nothing: the accident 'knowledge' in the category of quality. This solidifies the concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن عبارة المحقق الدواني هكذا الأبيض إذا أخذ لا بشرط شيء فهو عرضي، وإذا أخذ بشرط شيء فهو الثوب الأبيض، وإذا أخذ بشرط لا شيء فهو العرض المقابل للجوهر، كما أن طبيعة الذاتي جنس ومادة باعتبارين ، وفصل وصورة باعتبارين فطبيعة العرض عرض وعرضي باعتبارين. وهذا تحقيق الفرق بين العرض والعرضي، لا ما يتخيل من أن الفرق بينهما بالذات، وهذا الكلام وإن دل على أن العرض والعرضي متحدان بالذات، لكن لا يدل على أن العرض والمحل متحدان بالذات، والذي يفهم منه أن الجنس والفصل
And know that the statement of al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawani is thus: 'White' when taken without condition is an accidental property (ʻaraḍī), and when taken with the condition of something is the white garment, and when taken with the condition of nothing is the accident (ʻaraḍ) opposite to substance. Just as the nature of the essential (dhātī) is genus and matter in two respects, and differentia and form in two respects, so the nature of accident (ʻaraḍ) is accident and accidental property in two respects. This is the verification of the difference between accident and accidental property, not what is imagined that the difference between them is by essence. This statement, although it indicates that the accident and the accidental property are united in essence, it does not indicate that the accident and the subject are united in essence. And what is understood from it is that the genus and differentia...
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the distinction between 'accident' (عرض) and 'accidental property' (عرضي), using the example of 'white' (الأبيض) as presented by the scholar al-Dawani. The author quotes al-Dawani's three-case analysis: 'white' when taken without any condition (لا بشرط شيء) is an accidental property predicated of a subject (e.g., 'this is white'); when taken with the condition of something (بشرط شيء) it refers to a concrete object like 'the white garment'; when taken with the condition of nothing (بشرط لا شيء) it is the accident category (quality) opposite to substance. Al-Dawani then analogizes: just as the nature of the essential (e.g., animal) can be considered as genus and matter in one respect, and as differentia and form in another, similarly the nature of accident can be considered as accident (category) and accidental property (predicate) depending on the mode of consideration. The author emphasizes that this clarifies the true difference between them: it is not an essential difference (i.e., they are not different categories by essence) but rather a difference in how the same nature is considered. However, the author cautions that this unity in essence applies to the accident and the accidental property, not to the accident and its subject (محل). For example, the quality 'whiteness' as an accident is not united in essence with the white body in the same way that the accidental property 'white' is predicated of the body. The final incomplete sentence suggests a further implication for genus and differentia. This passage corrects a common misconception that 'عرض' and 'عرضي' are distinct categories by essence; rather, they are the same quiddity considered under different conditions. An Islamic example: 'knowledge' is an accident (quality) in the category of quality; when taken absolutely, it is predicated of a knowing subject as an accidental property ('Zayd is knowledgeable').

Key Terms
عرض
عرضي
محل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They are different in essence
B) They are the same in essence but differ in mode of consideration
C) They are completely separate entities
D) One is a genus, the other is a species

Key Takeaway
Al-Dawani's modal distinction reveals that accident and accidental property share the same quiddity, differing only in how they are considered — with or without existential conditions.

?
Task 2: Consider the term 'justice'. Is it an accident or accidental property in the sentence 'The judge is just'? Explain using the modal distinction.

Three Views on the Composition of Essence
An analysis of three views on the composition of essence from genus and differentia.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the three positions on how genus and differentia compose the essence and identify why the union view (ittihad) is preferred.

Core Idea
Genus and differentia are not separate parts but conceptually distinct aspects of a single mental entity.

The question of how genus and differentia combine to form the essence of a species is a classic problem in Islamic logic. This passage presents three competing answers. Understanding this debate is crucial for grasping how definitions work, especially when evaluating definitions used in usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
Read the passage and list the three views: 1) joining as separate components, 2) no composition, only abstraction, 3) union into one entity.
The author dismisses the first and prefers the third.
2
Examine the objection to the first view
Note why the first view is called 'foolish': genus and differentia are 'predicated parts' (ajza' mahmula), not independent existents like physical parts.
Predicated parts are not real components; they are concepts we predicate of the species.
3
Understand the abstraction view
The second view says only the species exist as simple realities; genus and differentia are abstracted from them, like accidental universals. The difference is in the order of abstraction.
This view avoids separate parts but reduces genus and differentia to mental constructs.
4
Analyze the union view
The chosen view: genus and differentia unite into one thing (ittihad). Their distinction is only mental. The author says 'the existence of the genus is identical to the existence of the species mentally'.
This avoids both separate parts and mere abstraction, preserving the unity of essence.
5
Relate to real definitions
Apply this to a definition in fiqh or kalam. For example, define 'salah' as 'worship consisting of specific actions'. Here 'worship' (genus) and 'specific actions' (differentia) are not two separate parts; they are one definitional essence.
The union view explains how definitions are meaningful without circularity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: اختلفوا في كيفية تركيب الماهية من الجنس والفصل فقال بعضهم إنه بانضمام الفصل إلى الجنس وهما موجودان بوجودين وهو سخيف لأنهما من الأجزاء المحمولة. وقال بعضهم لا تركيب أصلا حقيقة إنما الموجود أنواع بسائط فالأجناس والفصول منتزعة منها كالعرضيات إنما الفرق بالانتزاع أولا وبالذات وثانيا وبالعرض. ومختار الشيخ الرئيس وغيره من المحققين أنه باتحاد الجنس والفصل وصيرورتهما شيئا واحدا واختاره المصنف وقال: وجود الجنس هو بعينه وجود النوع ذهنا ذهنا.
The second [discussion]: They disagreed about the manner of composition of the essence from genus and differentia. Some said it is by joining the differentia to the genus, both existing with two existences, and this is foolish because they are among the predicated parts. And some said there is no composition at all in reality; only the simple types exist, and the genera and differentia are abstracted from them, like accidental universals, the difference being by abstraction primarily and essentially, and secondarily and accidentally. And the chosen view of the Chief (Ibn Sina) and other investigators is that it is by the union of genus and differentia and their becoming one thing; and the author chose it, saying: the existence of the genus is identical to the existence of the species mentally exactly.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage discusses the metaphysical question of how genus and differentia combine to constitute the essence (mahiyya) of a species. Three positions are presented. The first, attributed to some, is that the differentia is added to the genus as a separate component, each with its own existence (wujudayn). The author dismisses this as 'foolish' (sakhif) because genus and differentia are 'predicated parts' (ajza' mahmula) – they are not separate existents like physical parts, but concepts predicated of the species. The second position is that there is no real composition; only the species exist as simple realities, and the genus and differentia are merely abstracted from them, like accidental universals (like 'white' extracted from a white object). The difference is in the manner of abstraction: genus and differentia are abstracted primarily and essentially, while accidents are secondary. The third, chosen by Ibn Sina (al-Shaykh al-Ra'is) and other investigators, and also by the author of this text, is that genus and differentia unite into one single entity (ittihad), not as two separate components. The author succinctly states: 'the existence of the genus is identical to the existence of the species mentally' – meaning the genus in the mind is the same as the species, but conceptually distinct. This is the preferred solution to avoid the circularity and other problems of the previous views.

Key Terms
انضمام (indimam): Joining or conjunction of separate parts; rejected as absurd for genus and differentia.
انتزاع (intiza'): Abstraction; the process of deriving a universal concept from a particular thing. [Note: In later philosophy, intiza' is often used for mental concepts that have no external counterpart.]
اتحاد (ittihad): Union; the genus and differentia become one entity in reality, distinguished only in the mind.
أجزاء محمولة (ajza' mahmula): Predicated parts; both genus and differentia are predicates of the species, not independent existents.
الشيخ الرئيس (al-Shaykh al-Ra'is): The epithet for Ibn Sina (Avicenna), whose view is followed here. [Note: In logic texts, this title is standard for Ibn Sina; later philosophers may refer to him as 'al-Shaykh' simply.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the chosen view, how do genus and differentia relate in the essence?
A) As two separately existing components joined together.
B) As two simple abstractions with no real distinction.
C) They unite into one entity, their distinction being mental only.
D) The differentia is added externally to the genus.

Key Takeaway
The correct view is that genus and differentia unite into one mental entity; they are not separate existents nor mere abstractions.
