Three Views on Genus-Differentia Composition
Author compares three views on composition and defends the union of genus and differentia.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three views on genus-differentia composition, refute the aggregation view, and explain the union view using the color example.

Core Idea
Genus is indeterminate; only union with differentia gives it actuality.

How do genus and differentia combine to form a species? This seemingly technical question has deep implications for how we define and understand quiddities. The author presents three competing views, rejects two, and defends the union view of Ibn Sina. This lesson will walk you through each view and the reasoning behind the preferred position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three views
List the three views: aggregation (differentia added to genus as separate existents), no composition (species are simple, genus and differentia are conceptual extracts), and union (they become one existence).
2
Understand rejection of aggregation
The author calls aggregation 'absurd' because genus and differentia are predicated parts—they cannot be separate existents. Ask yourself: why would separate existents make predication impossible?
3
Analyze the union view with the color example
Color is an indeterminate genus; it 'trembles' between being light-gathering or light-dispersing. Adding a differentia like 'blackness' does not create a second existent but actualizes the color. The differentia is the realizer (muḥaṣṣil) that removes indeterminacy.
The example shows how genus and differentia exist as one in the species, not as two things.
4
Examine the objection and response
Aggregation supporters argue union is impossible: after union, either both exist (distinct) or not (no union). The reply: they exist with one existence, so distinctness is denied. How does this answer the objection?
The passage ends with an unanswered 'if you say…'—this completes the dialectic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الثَّانِي): اختلفوا في كيفية تركيب الماهية من الجنس والفصل، فقال بعضهم: إنه بانضمام الفصل إلى الجنس، وهما موجودان بوجودين، وهو سخيف ؛ لأنهما من الأجزاء المحمولة وقال بعضهم: لا تركيب أصلا حقيقةً ، إنما الموجود أنواع بسائط، فالأجناس والفصول منتزعة منها، كالعرضيات، إنما الفرق بالانتزاع أولا وبالذات وثانيا وبالعرض ومختار الشيخ الرئيس وغيره من المحققين أنه باتحاد الجنس والفصل وصيرورتهما شيئا واحدا، واختاره المصنف وقال: (وُجُودُ الْجِنْسِ هُوَ ( بعينه (وُجُودُ النَّوْعِ ذِهْنَا (۲) ذِهْنَا(۲) وَخَارِجًا، فَهُوَ مَحْمُولٌ عَلَيْهِ فِيهِمَا ) ، لا كما ظن أصحاب الانضمام. (وَمَنْشَأُ ذَلِكَ أَنَّ الْجِنْسَ) ماهية متزلزلة ، (لَيْسَ لَهُ تَحْصِيلٌ (۱) قَبْلَ النّوع ، وَإِنْ كَانَتْ قَبْلِيَّةً لَا بِالزَّمَانِ) ، فلا بد من محصل يحصله، رافعا تزلزله، ومتحدا معه، فيصير نوعا محصلا، وقد عرفت تفصيله؛ (فَإِنَّ اللَّوْنَ مثلا إذا أَخْطَرْنَاهُ بِالْبَالِ فَلاَ يَقْنَعُ بِتَحَصُّل (۲) شَيْءٍ مُتَقَرِّرِ بِالْفِعْلِ)؛ لتزلزله في الوجود أنه بعينه قابض البصر أو مفرقه ، فكيف تقنع، (بَلْ يَطْلُبُ فِي مَعْنَى اللَّوْنِ بين زِيَادَة (۳) ، بأن يصير اللون محصلا معها ؛ حَتَّى يَتَقَرَّرَ (٤)) اللون | بِتَقَرُّرِهِ ] (٥) ويرتفع تزلزله (بالفعل). وبرهان تحقق هذه الماهيات أن العقل إذا لاحظها من حيث هي وجد نسبة التقرر واللاتقرر إليها متساوية، والجاعل جواد، فلا بد أن يفيض عليها الوجود، وإذ الوجود من غير الاتحاد ممتنع عليها، عليها، فلا الفصل ، وهذا لا يسكت المناظر ، بل لا يكاد يتم. قال أنصار التركيب الانضمامي إن اتحاد الاثنين باطل؛ لأنهما بعد الاتحاد إن كانا موجودين فهما متمايزان، فلا ،اتحاد، وإن كانا معدومين موجود والآخر معدوم فلا اتحاد أيضا، وأجيب أنهما موجودان بوجود واحد، فالامتياز ممنوع . فإن قلت: الوجود يتعدد بتعدد المضاف إليه ؟
(Second): They differ regarding the manner of composition of the quiddity from genus and differentia. Some said: it is by the aggregation of the differentia to the genus, both existing with two existences, which is absurd because they are among the attributed parts. Some said: there is no composition at all in reality, rather existents are simple species, so genera and differentia are extracted from them like accidents, the difference is only by extraction, primarily and essentially, and secondarily and accidentally. And the choice of the Chief (Ibn Sina) and other verifiers is that it is by the union of genus and differentia and their becoming one thing, and the author chose it and said: 'The existence of the genus is identical to the existence of the species, mentally and externally, so it is predicated of it in both', not as the supporters of aggregation thought. And the origin of that is that the genus is a trembling quiddity, having no realization before the species, though the priority is not temporal, so a realizer is needed to realize it, removing its trembling, and united with it, so it becomes a realized species, and you have known its detail. For example, color, when we bring it to mind, it is not satisfied by the realization of something actually determined, because of its trembling in existence: is it essentially light-gathering or light-dispersing? So how can you be satisfied? Rather, it seeks in the meaning of color an added meaning, so that color becomes realized with it, until color becomes determined by its determination and its trembling is removed actually. And the proof of the realization of these quiddities is that the intellect, when it observes them as such, finds the relation of determination and non-determination to them equal, and the giver is generous, so existence must overflow upon them; and since existence without union is impossible for them, they must unite with the differentia. And this does not silence the opponent, rather it barely completes. The supporters of aggregative composition said: the union of two things is invalid, because after union, if they both exist then they are distinct, so no union; if both are non-existent or one exists and the other is non-existent, then also no union. And it was answered: they exist with one existence, so distinctness is denied. If you say: existence multiplies by the multiplicity of that to which it is attributed?
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage begins by enumerating three views on how a quiddity is composed from genus and differentia. The first view (aggregation) is rejected as absurd because genus and differentia are predicated parts, not separable existents. The second view (no composition) holds that reality consists of simple species, and genera and differentia are mere conceptual extracts. The third view (union) is preferred: genus and differentia unite into one existence, both mentally and externally. The author illustrates this with color: the genus 'color' is indeterminate (trembling) until united with a differentia like 'blackness' to become a realized species. A proof follows: the intellect sees that such quiddities are indifferent to existence/non-existence; the generous Creator gives existence, which requires union with a differentia. The passage then presents an objection from aggregation supporters: union of two things is impossible because either both exist (distinct) or not (no union). The reply is that they exist with one existence, so no distinctness. The passage ends with a further objection (if you say existence multiplies) which remains unanswered in this window.

Key Takeaway
The union view solves the problem of composition by making genus and differentia existentially one, preserving predication and actualizing the species.

Refuting the Aggregation Objection to Union
Dialectical refutation of the aggregation composition objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the debate between aggregation and unitary composition theorists and explain how the concept of a single existence resolves the objection.

Core Idea
Union of genus and differentia is possible because they share one existence, not two.

This passage presents a key ontological debate: how can two distinct concepts (genus and differentia) unite to form a single essence? Aggregation theorists argue that union is impossible because two things cannot truly become one. Unitary composition theorists respond by introducing the concept of a single existence (wujūd wāḥid). Understanding this argument is crucial for grasping how definitions (hadd) are constructed in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The aggregation theorists present a dilemma: after union, if both parts exist, they remain distinct; if both are non-existent or only one exists, there is no union. So union is impossible.
This is a classic dilemma argument.
2
Extract the answer
The unitary composition theorists answer: the two parts exist with a single existence (wujūd wāḥid), so they are not distinct. Distinctness is denied.
Single existence means the parts become one entity, not two.
3
Analyze the counter-objection
The objector then raises: existence is multiplied by the multiplicity of its subject (muḍāf ilayh). If each part has its own subject, they would have two existences.
This challenges the idea of a single existence.
4
Understand the final refutation
The author responds: existence is an accident (ʿaraḍ) that occurs to both parts together, and they have become one. So the existence is not multiplied; it belongs to the unified whole.
This refutes both the aggregation objection and a mistaken view among some unitary theorists who treated existence as an accident to two separate things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أنصار التركيب الانضمامي إن اتحاد الاثنين باطل؛ لأنهما بعد الاتحاد إن كانا موجودين فهما متمايزان، فلا اتحاد، وإن كانا معدومين أو أحدهما موجود والآخر معدوم فلا اتحاد أيضا، وأجيب أنهما موجودان بوجود واحد، فالامتياز ممنوع . فإن قلت: الوجود يتعدد بتعدد المضاف إليه ؟ قلت: الوجود عرض لهما، وهما صارا واحدا، ومن ههنا بطل مذهب بعض أصحاب التركيب الاتحادي: أن الوجود عرض للاثنين، هذا، والله أعلم بالصواب .
The partisans of aggregation composition said that the union of two things is invalid: because after union, if both exist, they are distinct, so there is no union; if both are non-existent, or one exists and the other does not, then there is no union either. It was answered: they both exist with a single existence, so distinctness is denied. If you say: existence is multiplied by the multiplicity of that to which it is attributed? I say: existence is an accident to them, and they have become one. From this, the view of some proponents of unitary composition—that existence is an accident to the two—is invalidated. This, and God knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This dialectical passage presents an objection from the partisans of aggregation composition (tarkīb inḍimāmī) against the possibility of union (ittiḥād) between genus and differentia in unitary composition (tarkīb ittiḥādī). The objection is a dilemma: after union, if both parts exist, they must remain distinct, so no true union; if both are non-existent or only one exists, there is no union either. The answer refutes the premise that they are distinct: they exist with a single existence (wujūd wāḥid), so distinctness (imtiyāz) is denied. The objector then raises a counter-point: existence is multiplied by the multiplicity of its subject (muḍāf ilayh). The response distinguishes: existence is an accident (ʿaraḍ) that occurs to both parts together, and they have become one entity. This refutes the mistaken view of some unitary composition theorists who held that existence is an accident to two separate things. The argument is resolved in favor of the unitary composition view that genus and differentia unite to form a single actual existence, like 'living being' and 'rational' uniting in 'human'.

Three Opinions on Unitary Composition and External Composition
Author classifies three opinions among unitary composition theorists regarding genus and differentia.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the three opinions among Islamic logicians regarding the relationship between unitary composition (tarkīb ittiḥādī) and external composition (tarkīb khārijī), and identify the arguments for each.

Core Idea
The relationship between genus-differentia composition and matter-form composition is contested, with three distinct positions.

In this passage, the author classifies the views on whether a thing that has unitary composition (genus and differentia uniting into one essence) can also have external composition (matter and form). This is not merely a theoretical puzzle—it affects how we define hylomorphic substances and whether they can be captured by a true definition (hadd). Let's unpack the three opinions and their justifications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first opinion
A group held that unitary composition does not combine with external composition. This is the choice of al-Sayyid al-Muḥaqqiq. Thus, what is composed of matter and form (i.e., has external composition) cannot have a genus and differentia.
2
Identify the second opinion
Another group held that unitary composition is concomitant with external composition. This is the position of most later scholars. They argued that a part of a thing does not differ mentally and externally (though this is dismissed as homonymy) and that genus and differentia require two principles of abstraction, because multiplicity cannot be abstracted from a single simple.
3
Identify the third opinion
A third group held that they combine without necessary concomitance. This is the position of the Shaykh (Ibn Sīnā).
The text cuts off here, but the classification is complete.
4
Analyze the second opinion's argument and response
The second opinion's argument that genus and differentia need two principles is met with the response that a simple can have multiplicity abstracted from it, as in the case of the Necessary Existent (Allah), from whom many attributes are abstracted.
This response undermines the necessity of having two distinct principles.
5
Apply to a concrete example
Consider the definition of 'human' as a rational animal. According to the first opinion, since human has external composition (soul and body), it cannot have a genus and differentia—contradicting standard logic. The second opinion would say it has both. The third says it can have both but they are not necessarily linked.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الثَّالِثُ) : اختلف أصحاب التركيب الاتحادي، فذهب جمع إلى أنه لا يجامع الخارجي، وهو مختار السيد المحقق، فما هو مؤلف من مادة وصورة أن يكون له جنس وفصل، وجمع إلى أنه ملازم له، وهو مختار أكثر يمتنع المتأخرين، واستدلوا تارة بأن جزء الشيء لا يختلف ذهنا وخارجا، وهذه من اشتراك الاسم، وتارة بأن الجنس والفصل لا بد لهما من مبدئين ينتزع منهما ؛ لامتناع انتزاع الكثرة عن الواحد ، وجوابه البسيط قد ينتزع منه الكثرة كالواجب . وذهب جمع إلى أنه يجتمع من غير تلازم، إليه ذهب الشيخ؛ حيث
The third [point]: The proponents of unitary composition differed. A group held that it does not combine with external [composition], and this is the choice of al-Sayyid al-Muḥaqqiq; thus what is composed of matter and form is impossible to have a genus and differentia. Another group held that it is concomitant with it, and this is the choice of most later scholars. They argued sometimes that a part of a thing does not differ mentally and externally, and this is from homonymy; and sometimes that genus and differentia must have two principles from which they are abstracted, because it is impossible to abstract multiplicity from one. Its answer: a simple may have multiplicity abstracted from it, like the Necessary [Existent]. And a group held that they combine without concomitance; this is the position of the Shaykh [Ibn Sīnā], where...
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage is a critical classification of three opinions among proponents of unitary composition (tarkīb ittiḥādī) regarding whether this type of composition is compatible with external composition (tarkīb khārijī) in the same thing. The first opinion (al-Sayyid al-Muḥaqqiq) holds that they are incompatible: a composite of matter and form cannot have a genus and differentia. The second opinion (most later scholars) holds that they are concomitant (mulāzim): any unitary composite also has external composition. Their arguments: (a) a part of a thing does not differ mentally and externally—this is dismissed as homonymy (ishtirāk al-ism); (b) genus and differentia must have two principles (mabda’ayn) from which they are abstracted, because multiplicity cannot be abstracted from a single simple. The response to (b) is that a simple can have multiplicity abstracted from it, as in the case of the Necessary Existent (al-wājib) from whom many attributes are abstracted. The third opinion (Ibn Sīnā) is that they combine without necessary concomitance. The text cuts off, but the classification is complete. In Islamic thought, this relates to the question of whether substantial forms (like in hylomorphic composites) can be analyzed into genus and differentia.

Key Terms
التركيب الاتحادي
التركيب الخارجي

The Difficulty of Extracting Genus and Matter
Author explains difficulty of obtaining genus and matter

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the reciprocal difficulty of obtaining genus in composites and matter in simples using the operations of making determinate ambiguous and making ambiguous determinate.

Core Idea
Genus in composites is made ambiguous; matter in simples is made determinate.

This passage tackles a subtle problem: why is it hard to define a composite or a simple? The author shows that the same intellectual operation—making ambiguous or determinate—works in opposite directions. Understanding this helps you appreciate why definitions of spiritual beings (simple) and physical objects (composite) require different logical care.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish simple vs. compound in existence and intellect
Note that something simple in external existence (like the soul) can be compound in intellectual analysis. The author warns against confusing this with mutual entailment.
Simple in existence does not mean simple in concept.
2
Identify the two difficulties
In composites, obtaining the genus (ambiguous) is hard; in simples, obtaining the matter (determinate) is hard. The genus becomes ambiguous only by making the determinate matter ambiguous; matter becomes determinate only by making the ambiguous genus determinate.
This reciprocal operation is a key insight.
3
Understand the intellectual operation
Making the determinate ambiguous (in composites) and making the ambiguous determinate (in simples) is described as 'great and difficult for the intellect'. Reflect on why this is challenging.
The operation is not mechanical but requires insight.
4
Connect to the genus/matter and differentia/form analogy
The author concludes that genus is derived from matter and differentia from form. This is because genus represents the material aspect and differentia the formal aspect.
This is a classic link between logic and metaphysics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البسيط في الخارج بسيط عقلا، فمن فهم من هذه العبارة التلازم فقد غفل. لَكِنْ فِي الْمُرَكَّب تَحْصِيلُ مَعْنى الْجِنْسِ الْمُبْهَمِ عَسِيرٌ دَقِيقٌ، وَفِي الْبَسِيطِ تَنْقِيحُ الْمَادَّةِ وَوُجْدَانُهَا مُتَعَسِّرٌ وَمُشْكِلٌ جِدًّا؛ فإن الجنس إنما يحصل في المركب بإبهام المتعين الذي هو المادة والمادة إنما يحصل في البسيط يتعين المبهم الذي هو الجنس، فَإِنَّ إِبْهَامَ الْمُتَعَيِّنِ وَتَعْيِينَ الْمُبْهَمِ أَمْرٌ عَظِيمٌ عسير عند العقل. وَهَذَا هُوَ الْفَرْقُ بَيْنَ الْفَصْلِ وَالصُّورَةِ. فهذا إن أخذ بشرط عدم الزيادة فيه، واتحادها معه صورة للمركب، وعلة وجزء له مستحيل الحمل عليه، وإن كان نوعا بالنظر إلى أفراده كالصورة الجسمية، وإن أخذ بشرط الزيادة فهو نوع، وإن أخذ لا بشرط شيء فهو الفصل المحمول على الجنس، وعلى المجتمع من كل مادة وصورة. وَمِنْ هَهُنَا تَسْمَعُهُمْ يَقُولُونَ: إِنَّ الْجِنْسَ مَأْخُوذُ مِنَ الْمَادَّةِ، وَالْفَصْلَ مَأْخُوذُ مِنَ الصُّورَةِ.
The simple is simple in external existence but compound in intellect. Whoever understands from this expression mutual entailment has been negligent. However, in the composite, obtaining the meaning of the ambiguous genus is difficult and subtle; and in the simple, refining the matter and finding it is arduous and highly problematic. For the genus is only obtained in the composite by making ambiguous the determinate thing, which is matter; and matter is only obtained in the simple by making determinate the ambiguous thing, which is the genus. Indeed, making the determinate ambiguous and making the ambiguous determinate is a great matter, difficult for the intellect. And this is the difference between the differentia and the form. So this, if taken with the condition of no addition in it and its identity with it, is the form for the composite, and a cause and part of it, impossible to be predicated upon it; though it is a species with respect to its individuals, like the corporeal form. If taken with the condition of addition, it is a species; if taken without any condition, it is the differentia predicated upon the genus and upon the composite of every matter and form. And from here you hear them saying: the genus is taken from matter, and the differentia is taken from form.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and explanatory commentary on the text of Sullam al-Ulum. The author begins by clarifying that a thing simple in external existence (like the soul) is compound in intellect, and warns against misunderstanding this as a mutual entailment between simplicity and composition. He then explains the difficulty: in composites, obtaining the genus (which is ambiguous) is hard because the genus must be extracted by making the determinate matter ambiguous; in simples, obtaining the matter is hard because one must determine the ambiguous genus to find the material component. This reciprocal operation is intellectually demanding. This leads to the distinction between differentia (fisal) and form (sura): the same thing can be considered in three ways. When taken with the condition of no addition and inseparable identity, it is the form of the composite and cannot be predicated of the composite. If taken with the condition of addition (i.e., as a species alongside others), it is a species. If taken without any condition (la bi-shart shay'), it is the differentia that can be predicated of the genus and of any composite. The author then notes that this explains why logicians say genus is derived from matter and differentia from form: genus represents the material aspect, differentia the formal aspect.

Key Terms
فصل (fasl): The differentia that distinguishes a species from other species within the same genus; taken without condition (la bi-shart shay') it is predicated of the genus. [Note: In earlier works (e.g., Isaghuji), fasl is simply the essential differentia; the tripartite condition analysis is a later refinement in sharh literature.]
صورة (sura): The form that, when taken with the condition of no addition and identity with the thing, is a part of the composite and not predicated of it; also a species relative to its individuals. [Note: No significant shift; standard usage in Islamic philosophy.]
بشرط لا (bi-shart la): With the condition of absence; here, taken with the condition that nothing additional enters into the concept. [Note: This term is consistent across the tradition as a logical modality.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The genus is too general
B) The genus must be made ambiguous from a determinate matter
C) The genus is hidden in the form
D) The genus is the same as the species

Key Takeaway
The reciprocal operations of making determinate ambiguous (for genus in composites) and making ambiguous determinate (for matter in simples) are intellectually demanding and central to definition.

Three Conditions: Differentia vs. Form
Author distinguishes differentia from form using three conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a given essential attribute functions as a differentia (fasl) or a form (sura) by applying the three conditions: bi-shart la, bi-shart shay', and la bi-shart shay'.

Core Idea
The same attribute is form, species, or differentia depending on how it is conditioned.

The author now gives a precise logical tool: the very same essential attribute can be considered in three ways. Depending on whether we take it with the condition of no addition, with the condition of addition, or without any condition, it becomes form, species, or differentia. This distinction is crucial for understanding how definitions work and why genus is linked to matter and differentia to form.

Guided Path
1
Identify the 'this' being considered
The author says 'هذا' pointing to the essential attribute that distinguishes the species. This is the same entity that can be viewed under three conditions.
The attribute is like 'natiq' (rational) for humans.
2
Apply the condition 'bi-shart la' (with condition of no addition)
If taken with the condition that nothing additional is included, and it is identical with the composite, then it is the form. It is a part of the composite and cannot be predicated of the composite (e.g., 'natiq' as form cannot be said 'human is natiq' because that would be predication of a part).
This is the form (sura) in the composite.
3
Apply the condition 'bi-shart shay' (with condition of addition)
If taken with the condition that there is addition (i.e., other attributes or individuals), it becomes a species. For example, 'natiq' with the addition of other attributes gives the species 'human'.
Under this condition, it is a species (naw').
4
Apply 'la bi-shart shay' (without any condition)
If taken without any condition, it is the differentia (fasl) that can be predicated of the genus and of the composite. For example, 'natiq' without condition is predicated: 'human is rational' and 'animal is rational'.
This is the differentia (fasl) in definitions.
5
Link to genus-matter and differentia-form
The author concludes that genus is taken from matter and differentia from form. This aligns with the conditions: matter provides the genus (ambiguous), form provides the differentia (determinate).
This reinforces the metaphysical grounding of logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البسيط في الخارج بسيط عقلا، فمن فهم من هذه العبارة التلازم فقد غفل. لَكِنْ فِي الْمُرَكَّب تَحْصِيلُ مَعْنى الْجِنْسِ الْمُبْهَمِ عَسِيرٌ دَقِيقٌ، وَفِي الْبَسِيطِ تَنْقِيحُ الْمَادَّةِ وَوُجْدَانُهَا مُتَعَسِّرٌ وَمُشْكِلٌ جِدًّا؛ فإن الجنس إنما يحصل في المركب بإبهام المتعين الذي هو المادة والمادة إنما يحصل في البسيط يتعين المبهم الذي هو الجنس، فَإِنَّ إِبْهَامَ الْمُتَعَيِّنِ وَتَعْيِينَ الْمُبْهَمِ أَمْرٌ عَظِيمٌ عسير عند العقل. وَهَذَا هُوَ الْفَرْقُ بَيْنَ الْفَصْلِ وَالصُّورَةِ. فهذا إن أخذ بشرط عدم الزيادة فيه، واتحادها معه صورة للمركب، وعلة وجزء له مستحيل الحمل عليه، وإن كان نوعا بالنظر إلى أفراده كالصورة الجسمية، وإن أخذ بشرط الزيادة فهو نوع، وإن أخذ لا بشرط شيء فهو الفصل المحمول على الجنس، وعلى المجتمع من كل مادة وصورة. وَمِنْ هَهُنَا تَسْمَعُهُمْ يَقُولُونَ: إِنَّ الْجِنْسَ مَأْخُوذُ مِنَ الْمَادَّةِ، وَالْفَصْلَ مَأْخُوذُ مِنَ الصُّورَةِ.
The simple is simple in external existence but compound in intellect. Whoever understands from this expression mutual entailment has been negligent. However, in the composite, obtaining the meaning of the ambiguous genus is difficult and subtle; and in the simple, refining the matter and finding it is arduous and highly problematic. For the genus is only obtained in the composite by making ambiguous the determinate thing, which is matter; and matter is only obtained in the simple by making determinate the ambiguous thing, which is the genus. Indeed, making the determinate ambiguous and making the ambiguous determinate is a great matter, difficult for the intellect. And this is the difference between the differentia and the form. So this, if taken with the condition of no addition in it and its identity with it, is the form for the composite, and a cause and part of it, impossible to be predicated upon it; though it is a species with respect to its individuals, like the corporeal form. If taken with the condition of addition, it is a species; if taken without any condition, it is the differentia predicated upon the genus and upon the composite of every matter and form. And from here you hear them saying: the genus is taken from matter, and the differentia is taken from form.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and explanatory commentary on the text of Sullam al-Ulum. The author begins by clarifying that a thing simple in external existence (like the soul) is compound in intellect, and warns against misunderstanding this as a mutual entailment between simplicity and composition. He then explains the difficulty: in composites, obtaining the genus (which is ambiguous) is hard because the genus must be extracted by making the determinate matter ambiguous; in simples, obtaining the matter is hard because one must determine the ambiguous genus to find the material component. This reciprocal operation is intellectually demanding. This leads to the distinction between differentia (fisal) and form (sura): the same thing can be considered in three ways. When taken with the condition of no addition and inseparable identity, it is the form of the composite and cannot be predicated of the composite. If taken with the condition of addition (i.e., as a species alongside others), it is a species. If taken without any condition (la bi-shart shay'), it is the differentia that can be predicated of the genus and of any composite. The author then notes that this explains why logicians say genus is derived from matter and differentia from form: genus represents the material aspect, differentia the formal aspect.

Key Terms
فصل (fasl): The differentia that distinguishes a species from other species within the same genus; taken without condition (la bi-shart shay') it is predicated of the genus. [Note: In earlier works (e.g., Isaghuji), fasl is simply the essential differentia; the tripartite condition analysis is a later refinement in sharh literature.]
صورة (sura): The form that, when taken with the condition of no addition and identity with the thing, is a part of the composite and not predicated of it; also a species relative to its individuals. [Note: No significant shift; standard usage in Islamic philosophy.]
بشرط لا (bi-shart la): With the condition of absence; here, taken with the condition that nothing additional enters into the concept. [Note: This term is consistent across the tradition as a logical modality.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under which condition is an essential attribute considered a differentia (fasl)?
A) Bi-shart la (with condition of no addition)
B) Bi-shart shay' (with condition of addition)
C) La bi-shart shay' (without any condition)
D) Always, regardless of condition

Key Takeaway
The same essential attribute functions as form, species, or differentia depending on whether it is taken with the condition of no addition, with addition, or without condition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the attribute 'haraka ikhtiyariyya' (voluntary movement) for defining 'insan' (human). Determine under which condition it would be: (a) form, (b) species, (c) differentia. Provide a brief justification for each.
?
The author says 'genus is taken from matter, differentia from form'. Explain this using the example of 'animal' (genus) and 'rational' (differentia) for humans, showing how the material aspect corresponds to the genus and the formal aspect to the differentia.

Defining Relative Species and the Condition of Primary Predication
Discusses definition of relative species and condition of primary predication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the definition of relative species and evaluate the condition of primary predication in distinguishing it from sub-species.

Core Idea
Primary predication seems to exclude true species as well – so it is not a safe criterion.

In this passage, the author critically examines the definition of relative species (النوع الإضافي), especially the condition that it be predicated primarily (قول أولي). This condition was added to exclude sub-species, but al-Sayyid objects that it also excludes man from being a species of remote genera. Let's unpack the definition and the critique.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
What is the definiendum? The relative species (النوع الإضافي), a universal that is itself a species of something and a genus of something else.
2
Extract the proposed definition
The passage gives: 'relative species is a universal predicated in answer to what is it such that it and others have the genus predicated in answer to what is it.' This includes the condition that it is predicated primarily (قول أولي) to exclude sub-species.
3
Understand the condition of primary predication
Primary predication means the predicate is said of the subject directly, not through an intermediary. The condition is meant to exclude sub-species, which are not predicated primarily.
4
Analyze al-Sayyid's objection
Al-Sayyid argues that if primary predication is required, then man is not a species of body or substance because body is predicated with a middle term (through a long chain of genera). Thus the condition is too restrictive.
5
Consider the alternative
Al-Sayyid suggests dropping the primacy condition and excluding sub-species by another condition, such as that the species is predicated essentially while genus is predicated through it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْإِضَافِيُّ ، وَبَيْنَهُمَا عُمُومٌ مِنْ وَجْهِ ؛ لتصادقهما في الأنواع المركبة، وتفارق الأول من الثاني في الأنواع البسيطة، كالوجود، وتفارق الثاني من الأول في الأجناس المركبة، كالحيوان، هذا ما عليه المتأخرون . (وَقِيلَ) : بينهما عموم وخصوص (مُطْلَقًا) ، هذا ما عليه القدماء. قال في الصنف - ثم إضافة قيد قولا أوليا مما صنعه صاحب الشمسية (۳۱۸/۱)، وشارحه القطب (۳۲۲/۱)، ولكن السيد في حواشيه قال: هذا القيد ـ أي قيد «قولا أوليا» ـ وإن أخرج عن الحد أخرج النوع عنه أيضا بالقياس إلى الأجناس البعيدة، فيلزم أن لا يكون الإنسان نوعا للجسم النامي ولا للجسم ولا للجوهر، مع أنه يسمى نوع الأنواع؛ لكونه نوعا لكل واحد من الأنواع التي فوقه، وأيضا النوع لما كان مضايفا للجنس فإذا اعتبر في النوع القول الأولي فلا بد من اعتباره في الجنس أيضا ، وإلا لم يكن مضايفا له، فيلزم أن لا تكون الأجناس البعيدة أجناسا للماهية التي هي بعيدة بالقياس إليها، فالأولى أن يُترك قيد الأولية، ويُخْرَج الصنف بقيد آخر ، ويقال : النوع الإضافي كلي مقول في جواب ما هو ، يقال عليه وعلى غيره الجنس في جواب ما هو، ووافقه الدواني في شرح التهذيب به (١/١٥). بينما قال الشارح في منهيته (٨٤) موجها لصنيع المصنف: «قوله (خرج الصنف) هذا على أنه أريد بالماهية الأمر المعقول، وخرج به الشخص؛ لأنه غير معقول، بل إنما يحصل علمه بالحواس ، وأما الصنف فلكونه كليا أمر معقول، فيدخل في الماهية، فيخرج بقيد الأولية؛ فإن المحمول عليه بالذات النوع، والجنس محمول بالواسطة ، واعترض على إخراج الشخص بأن الشخص أيضا قد يصير معقولا كالأشخاص المجردة ، لكن هذا غير ضار ؛ فإنه بعد تسليم أن المعقول منه شخص يخرج بقيد الأولية. وأما إن أريد بالماهية المقول في جواب ما هو فلا يدخل في الماهية الشخص والصنف، فلا حاجة إلى قيد زائد». وأما قوله أنه أريد بالماهية الأمر المعقول، أي الأمر الكلي، صرح به الدواني في شرح التهذيب (١٤ / ب ، (۱/۱٥) ، وتبعه اليزدي ـ حيث أهمل السعد في التهذيب قيد الأولية - فلأن الماهية تدل التزاما على الكلية»، كما أفاده الدواني. لقد أجاد وأفاد وأوجز المحقق اليزدي في شرح التهذيب في هذا المقام، كما فراجعه إن شئت بتريث.
The relative (species), and between them there is generality from one aspect; because they coincide in composite species, and the first differs from the second in simple species, like existence, and the second differs from the first in composite genera, like animal. This is the position of the later scholars. And it was said: between them there is absolute generality and specificity. This is the position of the early scholars. He said concerning the sub-species: then adding the condition 'primary predication' is what the author of al-Shamsiyya (1/318) and its commentator al-Qutb (1/322) did. But al-Sayyid in his glosses said: this condition – i.e., the condition 'primary predication' – even if it excludes from the definition, it also excludes species from it with respect to remote genera. It would follow that man is not a species of growing body, nor of body, nor of substance, although he is called the species of species, because he is a species for each of the species above him. Also, since species is correlative to genus, if primary predication is considered in species, it must be considered in genus as well; otherwise it would not be correlative to it. It would follow that remote genera are not genera for the quiddity that is remote with respect to them. So it is better to abandon the condition of primacy and exclude sub-species by another condition, and it is said: relative species is a universal predicated in answer to 'what is it' such that it and others have the genus predicated in answer to 'what is it'. Al-Dawwani agreed with him in his commentary on al-Tahdhib (1/15). Meanwhile, the commentator said in his versified commentary (84), justifying the author's action: 'His statement (he excluded sub-species) is based on the understanding that by quiddity is meant the intelligible thing, and by it the individual is excluded because it is not intelligible; rather its knowledge is obtained through the senses. As for sub-species, because it is universal, it is an intelligible thing, so it enters into the quiddity, and is excluded by the condition of primacy; for what is predicated essentially is species, while genus is predicated indirectly. It was objected to the exclusion of the individual that the individual may also become intelligible, like immaterial individuals, but this is not harmful; for after conceding that the intelligible from it is an individual, it is excluded by the condition of primacy. If by quiddity is meant what is predicated in answer to 'what is it', then the individual and sub-species do not enter into the quiddity, so no extra condition is needed.' As for his statement that by quiddity is meant the intelligible thing, i.e., the universal thing, this was stated explicitly by al-Dawwani in his commentary on al-Tahdhib (14/b, 1/15), and al-Yazdi followed him – where Sa'd in al-Tahdhib neglected the condition of primacy – because quiddity connotes universality, as al-Dawwani explained. The scholar al-Yazdi excelled, benefited, and abbreviated in his commentary on al-Tahdhib in this regard, so consult it if you wish deliberately.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the definition of relative species (النوع الإضافي). It begins by stating the later scholars' view that between genus and species there is generality from one perspective (عموم من وجه), giving examples: they coincide in composite species (e.g., 'animal' is both a genus for horse and a species for body) but differ in simple species like 'existence' (which is a genus but not a species) and composite genera like 'animal' (which is a species but also a genus for horse). The early scholars held absolute generality and specificity (عموم وخصوص مطلقا). Then the passage discusses the condition 'primary predication' (قولا أوليا) added by the author of al-Shamsiyya and al-Qutb to exclude sub-species (الصنف) from the definition of species. Al-Sayyid objects that this condition also excludes true species from being a species for remote genera, e.g., man would not be a species of body or substance, contradicting 'species of species'. He argues that since species and genus are correlatives, the condition should apply to both or neither. He suggests dropping the primacy condition and excluding sub-species by another condition. The commentator defends the author by distinguishing two meanings of quiddity: (1) the intelligible universal, in which case individuals are excluded because they are sensible, and sub-species are included but then excluded by the primacy condition; (2) what is predicated in answer to 'what is it', in which case neither individuals nor sub-species are included, so no extra condition is needed. The passage concludes by endorsing al-Yazdi's concise treatment.

Key Terms
النوع الإضافي (al-naw' al-idafi): A universal predicated of multiple things differing in species, which is itself a species of something else and a genus of something else, i.e., a subordinate species. [Note: Standard term across post-Abhari logic; no significant shift.]
قول أولي (qawl awwali): Primary predication: predication without intermediary, i.e., the predicate is said of the subject directly, not through a middle term. [Note: In earlier logic (e.g., Aristotle), this corresponds to immediate predication; in later Islamic logic, it becomes a technical condition in definitions.]
الصنف (al-sinf): Sub-species or subspecies: a universal that is predicated of species but not of individuals directly, like 'bird' for sparrow. [Note: In some texts, sinf is synonymous with naw' idafi; here it is distinguished by the condition of primary predication.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Sayyid's objection, why is the condition 'primary predication' problematic in defining relative species?
A) It excludes sub-species but not individuals.
B) It causes man to not be considered a species of body or substance.
C) It makes genus and species non-correlative.
D) It only applies to essential predicates.

Key Takeaway
The definition of relative species must carefully balance breadth and precision; the condition of primary predication was added to exclude sub-species but was found to also exclude true species, leading to a refined alternative.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. In Kalam, the definition of 'ilm (knowledge) is often given as 'a property through which a quiddity is distinguished'. Check if this definition satisfies the condition of primary predication for both the species of knowledge and its sub-types.
?
2. Given the definition of relative species as 'a universal predicated in answer to what is it, such that it and its genus are both predicated in answer to what is it', identify whether 'bird' (as a sub-species) would be excluded by the condition of primary predication.

The Scholarly Debate on the Condition of Primary Predication
Debate on the condition of primary predication across commentaries.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the layers of commentary on the definition of relative species, tracing how each scholar refined or defended the condition of primary predication.

Core Idea
Commentary reveals that logical definitions are not static but subject to cumulative critique and refinement.

This passage showcases a chain of commentators: the author of al-Shamsiyya introduces the condition 'primary predication'; al-Qutb expands; al-Sayyid objects; the commentator of the versified commentary defends; al-Dawwani and al-Yazdi offer further clarifications. Understanding this chain helps you see how Islamic logic evolves through debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base text
The author of al-Shamsiyya and al-Qutb added the condition 'primary predication' to the definition of relative species to exclude sub-species.
2
Identify the objection
Al-Sayyid (in his glosses) objects that the condition is too restrictive, as it excludes man from being a species of remote genera, and that it should also apply to genus.
3
Trace the defense
The commentator of the versified commentary defends the original by distinguishing two meanings of 'quiddity': (1) the intelligible universal, (2) what is predicated in answer to 'what is it'. In the first sense, individuals are excluded by being non-intelligible, while sub-species are included but then excluded by primacy. In the second sense, neither individuals nor sub-species are included, so no extra condition needed.
4
Note further endorsements
Al-Dawwani and al-Yazdi confirm this interpretation, with al-Yazdi's concise treatment praised.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Avicenna's Infinite Regress Doubt about Differentiae
Author presents the famous doubt about infinite regress of differentiae.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the infinite regress argument against the possibility of complete definition and identify its key steps.

Core Idea
If every differentia requires a differentia, no definition can be complete.

This passage presents a famous logical doubt from Avicenna's al-Shifa that challenges the very possibility of complete definition. The argument shows that each differentia, as a concept, falls under a universal that itself needs a differentia, leading to an infinite regress. Understanding this doubt is crucial for evaluating definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
The doubt claims that every differentia requires another differentia, leading to infinite regress. Locate this in the Arabic: the conclusion is 'li-kull fasl fasl' (for every differentia there is a differentia).
2
Break down the dilemma
Every differentia is a concept. Either it is the most general predicate (like 'thing' or 'existent') or it falls under a more general predicate. Show why the first horn is false: if it were the most general, it would not distinguish anything, and there is nothing more general than 'thing'.
3
Trace the regress
If the differentia falls under a more general predicate, it must be separated from other things sharing that predicate by another differentia. That second differentia itself is a concept under a universal, requiring yet another differentia, and so on ad infinitum.
4
Assess the consequence
The infinite chain means the quiddity (essence) of the species would be composed of an infinite number of things, which is impossible. This challenges the possibility of a finite, complete definition (hadd).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وههنا شك مشهور من وجهين، الأول: ما أورده في الشفاء وهو أن كل فصل معنى من المعاني، فإما أعم المحمولات أو تحته، والأول باطل، وإلا فلا يكون مميزا، وأيضا لا أعم من الشيء والموجود، فتعين الثاني، فهو منفصل عن المشاركات في ذلك العام بفضل آخر؛ لأن الانفصال لا يكون إلا بما يكون مميزا، وهكذا الكلام فيه، فإذن لكل فضل فصل، ويتسلسل، فيلزم تركيب الماهية من أمور غير متناهية.
And here there is a famous doubt from two aspects. The first is what he mentioned in al-Shifa: that every differentia is one of the meanings (concepts); either it is the most general of predicates or under it. The first is false, otherwise it would not be a distinguisher, and also there is nothing more general than thing and existent. So the second is determined. Thus it is separated from the things sharing that universal by virtue of another differentia, because separation only occurs by what is a distinguisher. And the same applies to it. So for every differentia there is a differentia, leading to infinite regress, and thus the quiddity would be composed of infinite things.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage presents a famous logical doubt about definition. It is attributed to Avicenna's al-Shifa. The argument proceeds as follows: Every differentia is a concept (معنى). Either it is the most general predicate (أعم المحمولات) or it falls under a more general predicate. It cannot be the most general because then it would not distinguish anything (since the most general is common to all). Also, the most general is 'thing' or 'existent', which cannot be a differentia. So the differentia must fall under a more general predicate. That more general predicate must be separated from its other instances by another differentia. That second differentia itself, being a concept, also falls under a more general predicate, requiring another differentia, and so on ad infinitum. This infinite regress means that the quiddity (essence) of the species would be composed of an infinite number of things, which is impossible. This doubt challenges the possibility of complete definition.

Key Terms
شك مشهور
أعم المحمولات
منفصل
يتسلسل

Checkpoint

A) Because differentiae are infinitely complex.
B) Because every differentia is a concept that must be distinguished from other concepts sharing its most general predicate, requiring a further differentia.
C) Because definitions are circular.
D) Because differentiae are always more general than the species.

Key Takeaway
The infinite regress argument shows that complete definition may be impossible, forcing us to rely on partial descriptions (rusum) or ostensive definitions.

Avicenna's Doubt on Infinite Regress of Differentiae and Its Solution
Author presents Avicenna's doubt on infinite regress of differentiae and provides a solution rejecting the assumption.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditional structure of Avicenna's infinite regress argument and evaluate the author's solution rejecting the assumption that every concept is distinguished by a differentia.

Core Idea
Infinite regress of differentiae is avoided by recognizing that concepts do not always require a differentia.

In this passage, the author presents a famous skeptical argument from Avicenna's al-Shifa that seems to force an infinite regress of definitions. The argument proceeds by a series of conditional steps. We will walk through each step, identify the underlying assumptions, and then see how the author resolves the doubt by rejecting a key premise. This is a classic example of logical deconstruction in Islamic philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim of Avicenna's doubt
Avicenna argues that every differentia, being a universal meaning, must be either the most general or under a more general. Since the most general cannot distinguish, it must be under a general, and to be distinguished from others sharing that general, it needs another differentia, leading to an infinite regress.
The claim is that the chain of differentiate cannot terminate.
2
Trace the conditional chain
The argument is structured as a series of conditionals: if every differentia is a meaning, then either it is the most general or under it. If it is under it, then it must be distinguished by another differentia. Apply this recursively, and you get an infinite regress.
Each step in the chain assumes that distinction from co-generics requires a differentia.
3
Locate the assumption being attacked
The author identifies the key assumption: that every concept must be distinguished from its partners in the more general by a differentia. This assumption is only valid if the more general is constitutive (muqawwim) of the concept. Since the differentia is simple, it does not have a constitutive general.
The author denies the universal applicability of the principle.
4
Examine the author's solution
The author states: 'We do not accept that every concept is distinguished from its partners in the more general by a differentia; that would only be necessary if that general were constitutive.' Because the differentia is simple, it is not subject to this requirement, so no regress follows.
The solution blocks the regress by denying a critical premise.
5
Apply the solution to a concrete example
Consider the differentia 'rational' for the species 'human'. It is under the category 'quality', but 'quality' is not constitutive of 'rational'; 'rational' is a simple concept. Therefore, it does not need another differentia to distinguish it from other qualities.
This shows the regress does not arise in practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَهَهُنَا شَكٌّ مَشْهُورٌ مِنْ وَجْهَيْنِ، الْأَوَّلُ: مَا أَوْرَدَهُ فِي الشِّفَاءِ وَهُوَ أَنَّ كُلَّ فَصْلٍ مَعْنًى مِنَ الْمَعَانِي، فَإِمَّا أَعَمُّ الْمَحْمُولَاتِ أَوْ تَحْتَهُ، وَالْأَوَّلُ بَاطِلٌ، وَإِلَّا فَلَا يَكُونُ مُمَيِّزًا، وَأَيْضًا لَا أَعَمَّ مِنَ الشَّيْءِ وَالْمَوْجُودِ، فَتَعَيَّنَ الثَّانِي، فَهُوَ مُنْفَصِلٌ مُمْتَازٌ عَنِ الْمُشَارِكَاتِ فِي ذَلِكَ الْعَامِّ بِفَصْلٍ آخَرَ؛ لِأَنَّ الِانْفِصَالَ لَا يَكُونُ إِلَّا بِمَا يَكُونُ مُمَيِّزًا، وَهَكَذَا الْكَلَامُ فِيهِ، فَإِذَنْ لِكُلِّ فَصْلٍ فَصْلٌ، وَيَتَسَلْسَلُ، فَيَلْزَمُ تَرْكِيبُ الْمَاهِيَّةِ مِنْ أُمُورٍ غَيْرِ مُتَنَاهِيَةٍ، وَالِاسْتِحَالَةُ عَلَى تَقْدِيرِ التَّلَازُمِ بَيْنَ التَّرْكِيبَيْنِ ظَاهِرٌ لِجَرَيَانِ الْبَرَاهِينِ، وَأَمَّا عَلَى تَقْدِيرِ نَفْيِهِ فَلَمْ يَدُلَّ دَلِيلٌ عَلَيْهَا، وَرُبَّمَا يُسْتَدَلُّ بِأَنَّهُ لَوْ جَازَ تَرْكِيبُ مَاهِيَةٍ مِنْ أُمُورٍ غَيْرِ مُتَنَاهِيَةٍ لَاسْتَحَالَ إِدْرَاكُهَا وَالتَّالِي بَاطِلٌ، وَلَا يَخْفَى مَا فِيهِ؛ فَإِنَّهُ إِنْ أَرَادَ اسْتِحَالَةَ الْإِدْرَاكِ بِالْكُنْهِ بِجَمِيعِ أَجْزَائِهِ تَفْصِيلًا، فَبُطْلَانُ التَّالِي مَمْنُوعٌ، وَإِلَّا فَالْمُلَازَمَةُ مَمْنُوعَةٌ قَطْعًا. وَحَلُّهُ: أَنَّا لَا نُسَلِّمُ انْفِصَالَ كُلِّ مَفْهُومٍ عَنِ الْمُشَارِكَاتِ فِي الْأَعَمِّ بِالْفَصْلِ، وَإِنَّمَا يَجِبُ لَوْ كَانَ ذَلِكَ الْعَامُ مُقَوِّمًا لَهُ، وَذَا مَمْنُوعٌ، فَإِنَّ الْفَصْلَ بَسِيطٌ. فَإِنْ قُلْتَ: أَلَيْسَ الْفُصُولُ مُنْدَرِجَةٌ تَحْتَ مَقُولَةٍ مِنَ الْعَشَرَةِ، وَالْمَقُولَةُ ذَاتِيٌّ لِمَا تَحْتَهُ؟ قُلْتَ: لَيْسَ يَجِبُ كَوْنُ كُلِّ مَقُولَةٍ ذَاتِيَّةً لِجَمِيعِ مَا يَصْدُقُ هِيَ عَلَيْهِ، وَإِنَّمَا هِيَ ذَاتِيَّةٌ لِأَنْوَاعٍ وَأَجْنَاسٍ مُرَكَّبَةٍ تَحْتَهُ. نَعَمْ، يَجِبُ أَنْ يَكُونَ الْمُمْكِنَاتُ يَصْدُقُ عَلَيْهِ شَيْءٌ مِنَ الْمَقُولَاتِ، وَلَوْ صِدْقًا عَرَضِيًّا، وَاللَّهُ أَعْلَمُ بِالصَّوَابِ.
And here is a famous doubt from two aspects. The first is what he mentioned in al-Shifa: that every differentia is a meaning from meanings, so either it is the most general of predications or under it. The first is false, otherwise it would not be distinguishing, and also there is nothing more general than 'thing' and 'existent'. So the second is determined. So it is separated, distinguished from the things that share in that general by another differentia, because separation is only by that which distinguishes. And so the discourse continues. So then every differentia has a differentia, leading to an infinite regress. So the essence would be composed of infinite things. Its impossibility, given the concomitance between the two compositions, is clear due to the proofs. But given the denial of that, no proof has been given. And perhaps it is argued that if a composition of infinite things were possible, its knowledge would be impossible, and the consequent is false. But what is in it is not hidden; for if he means the impossibility of essential knowledge of all its parts in detail, then the falsehood of the consequent is denied; otherwise, the implication is absolutely denied. And its solution: we do not accept that every concept is distinguished from its partners in the more general by a differentia; that would only be necessary if that general were constitutive of it, and that is denied, because the differentia is simple. If you say: Are not the differentiae subsumed under one of the ten categories, and the category is essential to what is under it? I say: It is not necessary that every category be essential to all things to which it is predicated; rather it is essential only to the composite species and genera under it. Yes, it is necessary that the contingent things have something predicated of them from the categories, even if accidental. And God knows best the truth.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage presents a famous skeptical argument from Avicenna's al-Shifa concerning the infinite regress of differentiae. The argument begins with a conditional: every differentia, being a meaning, is either the most general predication or under it. Since the most general cannot distinguish, the differentia must be under a general, and to be distinguished from others in that general, it needs another differentia. This leads to an infinite chain, implying that the essence is composed of infinite parts. The author then critiques the argument: the impossibility of infinite composition is not proven unless we accept a certain concomitant. Then a solution is offered: we do not accept that every concept needs a differentia to be distinguished from its co-generics; that is only required if the general is constitutive. Since the differentia is simple, no infinite regress follows. A subsidiary dialectical question about categories is answered by distinguishing between essential and accidental predication.

Does Absolute Existence Affect Essential Properties? The Dawani Position
Author presents and critiques the view that absolute existence has a necessary role in essential properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct al-Dawani's argument that essential properties are made by the essence and critique the opposing view that absolute existence is necessary for essential properties.

Core Idea
Necessity itself is uncaused; essential properties need not depend on existence.

In this lesson, we engage with a key dialectical issue in Islamic ontology: whether absolute existence (muṭlaq al-wujūd) has a necessary role in the essential properties (lawāzim al-māhiyya) of things. The author rejects the claim, arguing that necessity is not caused, but examines a nuance: some necessities are intrinsic, others from another. He then introduces al-Dawani's position that properties are made by the essence itself. This debate refines our understanding of the relationship between essence, existence, and attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
The passage begins with a question: 'Does absolute existence have a necessary role in essential properties?' A group says yes, because the essence is a cause and only exists when it causes. The author says no: 'the truth is that it has no role.'
The opposing view is that existence must be present for the essence to necessitate its properties.
2
Analyze the author's argument against the necessity of existence
The author argues that necessity itself (al-ḍarūra) is not caused. If one means no necessity is caused (essential or accidental), that is true. But if one means only essential necessity, the author denies that the property's necessity is essential—it could be from another.
This is a dialectical maneuver: he accepts a part of the opponent's premise (necessity uncaused) but distinguishes types of necessity.
3
Examine al-Dawani's alternative
Al-Dawani holds that essential properties are 'made' (majʻūla) by the making of the essence itself. Similarly, the subsistence of a thing for itself (thubūt al-shay' li-nafsihi) is not caused by existence.
This is a constructive position that shifts the explanatory burden from existence to the essence.
4
Reconstruct the refined position
The truth (al-ḥaqq) is that absolute existence has no necessary role. Necessity, especially essential necessity, is not caused by existence. The properties follow from the essence directly, not from its existence.
This aligns with later Ashʿarī and Ṣadrian positions that essence is prior to existence in quiddity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هل لمطلق الوجود دخل ضروري في لوازم الماهية؟ فقال جمع: نعم ؛ إذ الملزوم موجب ، وما لم يوجد لم يوجب ، وَالْحَقُّ لَا دخل له ؛ فَإِنَّ الضَّرُورَةَ لَا تُعَلَّلُ ، حَتى يَجِبَ وُجُودُ الْعِلَّةِ أَوْ لَا، واللزوم ضرورة، وفيه نظر ؛ فإنه إن أراد الضرورة لا تعلل، سواء كانت ذاتية أو غيرية فذلك قطعا، وإن أراد الذاتية فقط، فكون اللزوم كذلك ممنوع، بل يجوز أن يكون لزوم اللوازم كلها للممكنات من الغير، وقد ذهب المحقق الدواني وأتباعه إلى أنها مجعولة بجعل الملزوم ، وكذا ثبوت الشيء لنفسه .
Does absolute existence have a necessary role in the necessary properties of the essence? A group said: Yes, because the thing necessitated (the essence) is an efficient cause, and what does not exist does not necessitate. But the truth is that it has no role; because necessity is not caused, such that the existence of the cause would be necessary or not. And the necessity (of the property) is a necessity, and there is a discussion in it: If he means that necessity is not caused at all, whether essential or accidental, then that is certain; and if he means only essential necessity, then that the property is such is denied; rather, it is possible that all the necessary properties of possibles are from another. And the investigator al-Dawani and his followers have held that they are made by the making of the thing necessitated, and likewise the subsistence of a thing for itself.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange regarding whether absolute existence (mutilaq al-wujud) has a necessary role in the essential properties (lawazim al-mahiyya). A group holds that it does, because the essence (malzum) is a cause (mujib) and only exists when it causes its properties. The author rejects this, stating that the truth is that existence has no role, because necessity itself (al-darura) is not caused. He then examines the argument: if one means that no necessity is caused, whether essential or accidental, that is correct; but if one means only essential necessity, then it is denied that the property is essentially necessary—it could be from another cause. The author references al-Dawani and his followers, who hold that essential properties are made (maj'ula) by the making of the essence itself, and similarly the subsistence of a thing for itself. This passage clarifies the nuanced position on the relation between existence and essential attributes, rejecting the view that existence must be a cause.

Key Terms
مطلق الوجود
لازم الماهية
الضرورة
مجعولة

Key Takeaway
The key logical move is distinguishing between the necessity of a property being essential and its being caused by existence; al-Dawani's position grounds properties in the essence itself.

Three Considerations of the Natural Universal
Author explains the three ways the natural universal is considered: abstract, mixed, and absolute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three considerations of the natural universal (abstract, mixed, absolute) and identify which condition each imposes on the essence.

Core Idea
The natural universal is three things depending on which conditions you attach.

In this passage, the author introduces a key distinction in how we consider universal natures: either stripped of all accidents, mixed with some, or considered absolutely. Understanding these three modes is essential for grasping later discussions on existence and quiddity in Islamic philosophy and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three conditions
Note that the passage lists three key phrases: 'bi-shart la shay'' (with the condition of nothing), 'bi-shart shay'' (with the condition of something), and 'la bi-shart shay'' (without any condition). These correspond to the three considerations.
2
Map each condition to its term
Match each condition to the Arabic label: 'mujarradah' for the first, 'makhlutah' for the second, 'mutlaqah' for the third. Understand that 'mujarrad' means abstracted from all accidents, 'makhlut' means mixed with some accidents, and 'mutlaq' means absolute, without any condition.
3
Understand what each condition entails
For 'abstract': we consider the essence as having no determination or accidents at all. For 'mixed': we consider it as having some determination or accident. For 'absolute': we consider it without stipulating either presence or absence of accidents.
4
Grasp the neutral nature of the absolute
The passage states that the absolute universal, considered in itself, is neither existent nor non-existent, nor any accident like unity or multiplicity. This neutrality is crucial: the essence (mahiyyah) in itself is not any of these, even though in external reality it always has some of them.
This does not mean the essence is something else; it means the essence as such has no inherent connection to those attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ الطَّبَعِيُّ لَهُ اعْتِبَارَاتٌ ثَلَاثَةٌ: بِشَرْطِ لَا شَيْءٍ، أَيْ بِشَرْطِ عَدَمِ التَّعَيُّنِ وَعَدَمِ سَائِرِ الْعَوَارِضِ، يُسَمَّى مُجَرَّدَةً، وَبِشَرْطِ شَيْءٍ، هُوَ التَّعَيُّنُ وَغَيْرُهُ مِنَ الْعَوَارِضِ، يُسَمَّى مَخْلُوطَةً، وَلَا بِشَرْطِ شَيْءٍ، مِنَ الْعَوَارِضِ وَعَدَمِهَا، يُسَمَّى مُطْلَقَةً، وَهِيَ مِنْ حَيْثُ هِيَ، مَعَ قَطْعِ النَّظَرِ عَنْ جَمِيعِ الْعَوَارِضِ وَاللَّوَاحِقِ الْخَارِجِيَّةِ، لَيْسَتْ فِي ذَاتِهَا مَوْجُودَةً وَلَا مَعْدُومَةً وَلَا شَيْئًا مِنَ الْعَوَارِضِ، مِنَ الْوَحْدَةِ وَالْكَثْرَةِ، وَإِنْ كَانَ فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ لَا يَخْلُو عَنْ بَعْضِهَا.
Then the natural universal has three considerations: with the condition of nothing—i.e., with the condition of absence of determination and absence of other accidents—it is called abstract; with the condition of something—i.e., determination and other accidents—it is called mixed; and without any condition of accidents or their absence—it is called absolute, and it (insofar as it is) with disregard of all accidents and external concomitants, is in its essence not existent, not non-existent, and not any of the accidents, such as unity and multiplicity, even though in actuality it is not free from some of them.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage elaborates on the natural universal by distinguishing three ways it can be considered in the mind. The first is 'bi-shart la shay'' (with the condition of no thing): we consider the essence stripped of all external determinations and accidents; this is called 'abstract' (mujarrad). The second is 'bi-shart shay'' (with the condition of something): we consider the essence together with some determination or accident; this is called 'mixed' (makhlut). The third is 'la bi-shart shay'' (without any condition): we consider the essence in itself, disregarding both accidents and their absence; this is called 'absolute' (mutlaq). The author then explains that the absolute natural universal, considered in itself (min haythu hiya), is neither existent nor non-existent nor any accident like unity or multiplicity, although in external reality it is never free from some of these. This is a key metaphysical point: the essence (mahiyyah) in itself is neutral with respect to existence and accidents.

Key Terms
مُجَرَّدَة
مَخْلُوطَة
مُطْلَقَة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The absolute consideration (mutlaq) is the essence qua essence, neutral to all accidents and existence.

The Five Universals: Peripatetic Argument for Matter and Critique
Author critiques Peripatetic view on the principle of distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the Peripatetic argument for matter in multiple individuals, identify the author's critique, and articulate the Illuminationist alternative regarding the principle of distinction.

Core Idea
The principle of distinction is not a separable accident but the quiddity itself after the Maker's act.

This passage opens the section on the five universals by presenting the Peripatetic claim that any quiddity with multiple individuals must have matter and varying dispositions. The author then critiques this view and offers the Illuminationist perspective. Understanding this debate is crucial for grasping how Islamic philosophers debate the nature of universals and individuation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Peripatetic claim
Find the main assertion: every quiddity with multiple individuals requires matter and differing preparations.
2
Extract the definition of 'mā bihi al-imtiyāz'
Peripatetics define it as a separable accident, distinct from the quiddity and its necessary accidents.
This term is the key to the debate.
3
Understand the author's two interpretations
The author offers two meanings: (1) the giver of identity (the Maker), or (2) the source preventing commonality.
Each leads to a different conclusion.
4
Recognize the Illuminationist alternative
The author sides with the Illuminationists: the quiddity itself becomes distinct through the Maker's act, so sharing and distinction are one.
5
Consider the additional possibility
The author suggests that the distinguishing principle might be a necessary accident inseparable from the individual, not needing matter.
This weakens the Peripatetic case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الكليات الخمس قال المشاؤون : كل ماهية متكثرة الأشخاص لا بد لها من مادة ، و اختلاف استعداداتها لأن الماهية مشتركة ، وكذا لوازمها، وما به الاشتراك غير ما به الامتياز، فلا يكون ماهية أو لازما، بل يكون عرضا مفارقا، وهو ممكن الزوال، ففيها قوة ، وهي من العوارض للمادة، ولا يخفى ما فيه ؛ فإنه إن أريد بما به الامتياز مفيدا لهذية، فقد عرفت أنه الجاعل التام، وإنما يحصل الامتياز الشخصي بالجعل، وإن أريد أمر به ينتزع الهذية أي منشأ منع الشركة ، فنختار أنها الماهية والماهية بنفسها مشتركة، وبعد جعل الجاعل ممتازة، فما به الاشتراك نفس ما به الامتياز من غير زيادة أمر فيه على ما هو رأي الإشراقية ، على أنا نقول : يجوز أن يكون ما به الامتياز مفارقا بالنظر إلى الماهية ، لازما للشخص غير ممكن الزوال، فلا يحتاج في عروضها إلى المادة، فتأمل فيه، والله أعلم.
Section on the Five Universals. The Peripatetics said: Every quiddity having multiple individuals must have matter and differing preparations, because the quiddity is common, and so are its necessary accidents. That by which sharing occurs is other than that by which distinction occurs. So it is neither a quiddity nor a necessary accident, but a separable accident, and it is possible of removal. So in it there is potentiality, and it is among the accidents of matter. And it is not hidden what is in it. For if what is meant by 'that by which distinction' is what gives identity (hūdhiyya), you have known that it is the complete Maker, and personal distinction only occurs by making. And if what is meant is something by which identity is derived, i.e., the source preventing commonality, then we choose that it is the quiddity itself. But the quiddity by itself is common, and after the making of the Maker it becomes distinct. So that by which sharing is the same as that by which distinction, without any additional thing, as is the view of the Illuminationists. Moreover, we say: It is possible that that by which distinction is separate with respect to the quiddity, necessary for the individual, not possible of removal. So it does not need matter for its occurrence. So reflect on that. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage opens a new section titled 'The Five Universals'. The Peripatetics are cited: any quiddity that appears in multiple individuals must have matter and varying dispositions. The reason is that the quiddity is shared, and its necessary accidents are also shared. The principle of distinction (mā bihi al-imtiyāz) must be something other than the quiddity and its necessary accidents—it must be a separable accident, which is subject to change, implying potentiality in the individual, which is a property of matter. The author critiques this: if 'that by which distinction' is taken as the giver of identity (hūdhiyya), that refers to the Maker (God). Personal distinction comes from divine making. If instead it means the source of preventing commonality (manʿ al-shirka), then the author sides with the Illuminationists: the quiddity itself, after being made by the Maker, becomes distinct. So the principle of sharing and the principle of distinction are the same thing without any additional component. The author further suggests that it is possible that the distinguishing principle is separate from the quiddity but is a necessary concomitant of the individual, not subject to removal. In that case, it does not require matter. The author invites reflection.

Key Terms
ما به الامتياز
عرض مفارق
الجاعل التام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the Illuminationist view on the principle of distinction (mā bihi al-imtiyāz)?
A) It is a separable accident requiring matter.
B) It is the same as the quiddity after being made by the Maker.
C) It is a necessary accident of the quiddity.
D) It is the Maker Himself.

Key Takeaway
The Peripatetic view that individuation requires matter is critiqued by the author, who argues that the principle of distinction is either the Maker or the quiddity itself, not a separable accident.
